RATING: 8
Worker placement with a railroad theme and very unforgiving exponential scoring.

The game plays over 6 rounds, scales very well from 2-4 players, and uses the classic worker placement mechanism. Each player has their own personal board which shows three major railway lines and an industry track; the goal is to progress on at least some of these tracks by moving up lower value rails in order to allow for forward movement of higher value rails. The industry track works differently in that there is no upgrading but instead lower points are paid out quicker and key bonuses can be unlocked earlier. The main rails also unlock bonuses and scoring multipliers but this usually takes some time to achieve. 

There are several different actions to choose from, including many rail movements (black, grey, brown, tan, and white which are listed in ascending value), money (which serves as a worker placement but is also required for some actions), train engines/industry cities (needed to score your rail tracks or advance your industry marker), engineers (this is one of the few innovative features of this game; there is a stack of engineers of which only a few enter a game and they can be used when on the board but once they are hired they serve as your own private, generally superior, worker location, as well they serve as the round counters and score endgame points), as well as a few other actions. 

Scoring occurs every round and it starts slowly but ramps up massively as players start to push their valuable train tracks forward.  This is where the game can be a turnoff to new players; final scores can differ by several hundred points(!) buts that's just how it works in this game.  If you can get past your first few games, it's surprisingly addictive and "fun" and it should be noted that all of the expansions really make the game shine by increasing variability significantly. 

Own the [boardgame=181693]German Railroads[/boardgame], and [boardgame=206944]American Railroads[/boardgame] expansions and the [boardgame=166410]Mini-expansion[/boardgame].

RATING: 10
[COLOR=#FFFFFF][BGCOLOR=#FF9933][b]aaj's FAVORITE WORKER PLACEMENT[/b][/BGCOLOR][/COLOR]
[i]tl;dr - Russian Railroads has little to no theme, but the puzzle that it presents you is exciting to solve anew every time. This is my favorite way to exercise my brain with some tough reps, alongside two or three other workout buddies. Highly recommended.[/i]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

How do I love thee, let me count the ways? My father-in-law recommended Russian Railroads to me, and I bought it tentatively, because it's rather heavy compared to what I normally play. Despite this, RR has blossomed into my favorite heavy game (with the possible exception of Terra Mystica) and I always relish a chance to dive deep into its inner workings. 

Russian Railroads is, at its core, a collection of point-scoring mechanisms; but unlike a Feld game, these pieces all come together into an engine-building experience that's even thematic. You start out very slowly, earning 3 points here, 4 points there, but by the end of the game your engine has been built to full potentially and you are harvesting whole swathes of points, pulling in 60, 80, even 100 or more points per round. 

People have called this the best 'pure' worker placement game and I'm compelled to agree. The board is completely open and your only obstacles are the other players. Russian Railroads will reward players who can evaluate what everyone else is doing and construct an engine that zigs when everyone else is zagging -- this will let you take spots that have gone unnoticed and sneak into a lead! 

The base game is interesting and exciting, but a few initial moves can be on rails (the engineer/gold/double workers spaces will always be among the first picks), and German Railroads, the first expansion, totally shakes that up. You now have variable player boards, that let you specialize your tracks to meet your particular strategy. This mitigates opponents blocking you a little bit, and opens up your options. The second module, coal, also provides more spaces on the board and more ways for you to earn points. 

Let's be clear: this is multiplayer solitaire. But it's multiplayer solitaire at its absolute best, and that's OK -- sometimes you just want a puzzly game where the winner is not the one with the biggest fists, but the most finely executed strategy, brought to completion with ruthless Russian (or German!) efficiency. Highly recommended. 

[COLOR=#FFFFFF][BGCOLOR=#66CC33][b] EXPANSION(s) OWNED: [/b][/BGCOLOR][/COLOR]

I enjoy the flexibility that they provide and they keep players from getting painted into a corner. RR can be a harsh game so this mitigates this a bit. Coal adds more spaces on the board and new ways to use the base structures of the game.

RATING: 7
3 plays / 3, 4 players

LIKE: Hard decisions, every option is good. Interesting mechanisms with the sliding rails. The personal board is tidy yet holds lots of information. Lots of strategies once you get to know the big combos.

DSLIKE: Unforgiving game, but that makes it interesting. Experienced players have a great advantage. First player has large advantage. Difficult to catch up when behind. Points go over 200, so counting can be fiddly and math heavy.

OVERALL: The game was quite abstract for a train game, but that doesn't bother me. Some scores compound round after round, so those are the ones to go after, making strategy limited to certain choices. After a few plays with some pointers, the game is growing on me.

RATING: 7.5
Its a Euro-worker placement game - and by that I mean it has resources in the form of tracks and workers that are used to take actions. The personal board that tracks your railroad building is first rate albeit nearly overwhelming with options. That's one of the reasons the game takes a bit of time to get off the table, but I don't think it overstays its welcome: there's alot of game to enjoy here. However, I do take pause with some aspects. As fun as the building rails system is - and how counter intuitive! - it still feels like once you play the game a few times, the surprises are essentially over. To be sure there's lots of things yet to explore at that point - but you know the paths of victory and what's left is nuances, not revolutions.

It is *very* good for all that - better with perhaps 3-4 and not 5 (the game just lasts too long). The components and general design are very polished. So I like it and would suggest playing at least a few more times.

RATING: 5
2014-08-11
Russian Railroads is a relatively simple worker placement and an euro at its purest. What I especially liked was that you score several hundred points in a game, which makes those little differences count. However, the fact that you score more than half of the points in the final scoring (after the 6th round) feels a bit stupid. In the end, the first five rounds matter very little, since basically you are just building your position for the last round point rush.  And building that position is not too difficult. 
 
What I would have liked to see is possibility for great plays in the early game as well. 

Update 2015-04-14

RATING: 9
Wow!  I like this one!  Pure worker placement for individualized benefits along 4 tracks on individual player boards.  The interactions among the disparate elements mean there are a few different directions to pursue and you will always, ALWAYS want to do more than you can.  WHICH IS AWESOME!

I was a little concerned with some of the spots on the central board being generally more powerful than others up until I realized that the real balance is in the opportunity cost of taking these spots before other, even more desirable ones.  That is, getting extra workers for a round is nice up until it costs you the spot(s) you wanted to use them on.  WHICH IS AWESOME!  MORE TENSION AND A META GAME!  WOOT!

So yeah, there's all this potential for screwage and meta gaming and intrinsic balance via opportunity costs.  And there are a few personal choices to be made and they really are rather significant.  WHICH IS ALSO AWESOME!  Also, each game will be at least a little different via the different engineers used.  I wish there was a bit more replay value, but maybe there's an expansion or two lurking in the wings.  Or maybe the engineers are enough?  NEED MORE PLAYS!

Rating has the potential to go down (or up, I suppose) after more plays but I don't know that it will.  I *really* like this game!

RATING: 10
2-4(4)

Edit: Okay, so this is playing like a 10 right now. I'm sure the lack of a randomness and the small amount of engineers / scoring cards will possibly make the game run out of steam, but it's currently really fun to play. Namely, progressing along the tracks feel like you're unlocking achievements and the scoring each round, seeing how your gameplay is ramping the game up. Going from six points a round to a hundred does feel satisfying and like the nice culmination of a game's worth of strategy.

---------------------------------------------------

Parts of it may eventually feel a bit scripted (grabbing Engineers and then money) or certain tracks might not be completely balanced (e.g., it seems that at least getting the bonus tokens from St. Petersburg is near mandatory) or the bonus tokens themselves might not be balanced and etc.

And, of course, this does become a bit of a concern as there is such little variability between games and no randomness after the setup (I guess maybe some uncertainty in not knowing which bonus cards have been removed as far as directing strategy). So there are certainly concerns regarding that and also the inability to kneecap a leader and etc.

Nevertheless, it was a very satisfying game to play. All of the components sort of mesh nicely and the whole scale of the game is pleasing and doesn't feel too artificially inflated. In some games the game will introduce something to artificially ramp things up (I think, quintessentially, the extra worker from Lords of Waterdeep fits this in a way to make later turns feel like more is going on). Obviously, the massive uptick in Russian Railroads is sort of artificially codified into the game, but it feels thematic and is rewarding.

Just a game that was lots of fun for me.

RATING: 7
Russian Railroads does have trains with numbers that denote how far they can travel, in the style of 18xx games.  There the similarity to 18xx ends and a fairly abstract optimisation game of development along four tracks - three of them railways, one industry - begins. A focus on one track over others provides the strategic options.  There's a hatful of opportunities to score victory points. Worker placement is the main mechanism and, as in the best games of this sort, throws up some real dilemmas of prioritisation. 

Russian Railroads is handsomely produced with artwork that adds a thick gloss of theme.

RATING: 7
Not entirely sure why this game is so highly ranked on BGG at the moment.  It seems like a nice enough "puzzle" wherein you use very straightforward worker placements to advance markers of different colors on your player card in order to earn victory points each round.  

Presumably, the thrill is in figuring out how to squeeze out the most VP's by manipulating different colored markers (track markers they say) better than the other players. Each player quietly does their manipulations on their boards with the other players not really watching.

Theme is strangely close to nonexistent in this game, really an afterthought. Interactivity feels nonexistent, and curiously, lower than your average worker placement game.  Still, it is sort of fun in a mellow way.  There might be some replayability here as you experiment with manipulating your markers from game to game, though actually, I cannot envision much fun in doing those experiments.  I might play one more time I suppose.   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is basically a "victory point salad" worker placement euro game with very pasted on theme. But it's fun and very tight. The tough decisions, small race game elements and clever blocking of opponents are the biggest pluses and I think the (point)engine building aspect is very satisfying. The components and art are great and even manage to bring some theme to the otherwise totally mechanically themeless (or at least the gameplay does not support the theme at all, especially the factory-line) game. Replayability is also a big issue as there is little randomness in the setup and the amount of strategic variance is not that great. Still, the game manages to be interesting every time it hits the table.

Fun factor: 4/5
Strategy vs. luck: 5/5
Mechanics/gameplay tied to the theme: 2/5
Component/art quality: 4/5
Replayability: 2/5

RATING: 7.5
My first impression is overall good. I like worker placement games and this one is not an exception. However there were a few minor problems I've had with this game. First of all - at least in my group - two first actions that were taken were "two coins" and "two temporary workers". I don't like when games have such must-take-first actions, those should depend on one's situation, and not be almost always the first thing to grab. Secondly, I didn't feel there was enough interaction. There are so many similar action places to choose from that you do not really feel like you're blocking someone. If you do, you do it as a side effect of an action you wanted to choose anyway. Due to that I feel like everybody was playing his own game with very little interaction with other players. I will give it more plays, however I wouldn't like it to turn into a multiplayer-solitaire optimization type of game, because I'd rather have more blocking options. Maybe as we know the game more we'll draft those engineers and other bonuses more consciously, more wary of other players' choices.

RATING: 8
This is a train-themed game designed by an 18XX designer. But it is absolutely not am 18XX game!
Russian Railroads is instead a resource-management worker-placement game.

The games lasts 7 rounds (6 in two-player), during which players must develop three different russian railroads. Each player develops his own railroad in his own player board: there is no common board for the tracks (there's no tracks at all to be placed!) as in other train-themed games. The common board has instead the action spaces (like in most worker placement games), that are exclusive (once used, it is not available until next round), and players take turns placing workers (some action spaces require more than one) until everybody passes.
Rinse and repeat for 6 (5) more rounds.

So, what about the game if everything sounds familiar?

The unusual crossover of theme and mechanics is intriguing per se, but there's more to it than what I previously said! 
1. The three different railroads can be developed with different colored tracks (I guess they symbolize various kind of luxury wagons offered to passengers). This is quite unique and the choices you're making are very tight.
2. Every railroad gives you access to different kind of developments and, since you can't do everything, you must specialize and different strategies seem to be available.
3. There's also another way to make points: by developing factories, and this is another possible way to go.

Overall, there's lots of interlocking parts in the game, and it's a great brainburner (about the same weight as Agricola) of a game.

RATING: 8
Pro's:

1) The points ramp up is quite spectacular, scoring a measly 5 points or something in the first round and then scoring 100+ at the last rounds is very fun.
2) The slow progression of the tracks feels great. While it isn't exactly like you are actually building the railroad thematically, it does feel great to progress each individual track or the industry track.
3) Plays very quickly for a game of that weight.
4) Aesthetically very pretty. Has one of my favourite colour schemes of any board game I own. Plus the little rail pieces are very cute.

Con's:

1) Playing sub-optimally will mean you lose quite badly. You are likely looking at a difference of 100 + points in some cases. While I don't think it is a major flaw, I think some players would feel significantly disheartened.
2) I always wish I could do more or progress more tracks in the game.

Not sure:

1) The scoring phase, I honestly don't know here. As it is both tied to the fun element of the ramp up and the not so fun element of teaching it. The game provides the little guide sheet, though players are so unsure that they are doing it correctly that I invariably have to double check their scores.

RATING: 10
This game is so brilliant, so amazingly fantastic!!! Now this is absolutely my favourite Euro game, best worker placement game of all times! And I have over 40 games in my colleaction, so I have a base to compare...

Russian Railroads plays really smooth, the rules are very intuitive and easy to explain. With people who didn't play it before it takes ~15 minutes to explain everything. So far everybody with whom I've played it enjoyed it the most among all of our games, and demanded to play more and more. Now the game has even became the favourite game of my wife, even though she is not a fan of strategic economy boardgames.

Russian Railroads is easy to understand and get used to, however it has extremely deep mechanism and huge amount of choices how you build up your strategy. You can win the game many many ways, and there are no standard ways for what you have to go for. 

Forget the simplicity of Carcassonne or the over-complication of Bora Bora. Forget about long long explanation of Agricola. Russian Railroads succeeded there, where the other games failed to balance: it can be simple to explain and understand the same time while it offers a huge variety of playing and multiple choices for your strategy, at the same time offering great fun for everybody.

RATING: 7
An enjoyable worker placement game with several ways to score points, but the point values feel imbalanced; therefore I'm not confident that there are multiple paths to victory.  Perhaps playing exclusively with experienced players will improve the balance.  The purple track seems too lucrative considering that no engine is needed; meanwhile, it takes a large effort to generate points from railroad construction (advance track marker, obtain engine, unlock next more valuable track (color), advance that track marker, repeat).  Also, the second path seems useless except for unlocking bonus  chits.

There is almost no luck in the game aside from the sequence/setup of the engineer tiles (which is public information) and the choices/mistakes made by opponents during the game.  My biggest complaint is that there is too much contention over turn order and the engineer tiles (partly because there are very few good actions).  Also, the engineers are probably too powerful... giving you private action spaces for workers and a chance at a large point windfall (40/20 for most/2nd most).  My secondary complaint (and partial compliment) is that the game is fairly unforgiving of mistakes and suboptimal moves; experienced players usually an and can be runaway leaders, making the game difficult to introduce to and enjoy with newbies.

RATING: 5.75
This game is as good as a worker placement game with very low interaction can get. It does give you a lot of options and rewards planning. 

On the bad side: It is quite unforgiving, does have a runaway-leader problem and is rather mathsy. There's no way to comeback after a bad start.

On the good side it manages to offer a variety of strategies and still remains mostly intuitive. The playtime is ok.

RATING: 4
Provisional rating, might change if I play it again (probably down to a 4 ...)

I can see why some people love this game - if you like worker placement and VP optimisation games, give it a try.

For me it is too abstract, I felt no real connection with the theme based on the mechanics. No idea why I have black track then brown then white, which score different (increasing points) nor why some things cost rubles and some don't.

All in all - nothing I haven't seen before and no desire to own, and little to play again.

But I would play it again, nothing here to completely turn me off.

** Update, and down to a 4 (as I predicted), really don't play this with AP prone players, there's too much analysis, and it rewards planning and having a strategy rather than react to what happens in game. All in all, many other games I'd rather play.

RATING: N/A
Pure worker placement and engine building, with a thin veneer of train theme.  Russian Railroads is a fascinating study on the worker placement mechanism, and how to build a game where all players start with the same stuff and rapidly wind up with different incentives and values for commonly available stuff.  

Ultimately, while I liked the single play of RR I had on yucata, this game always seemed to be a little too long, a little too opaque, and a little too rule heavy for my usual gaming partners, and I never had the courage to bring it out.  I think it's a great game, but there's no reason for me to own it.

RATING: 7
Feels like a pretty standard, well designed worker placement game. 
Almost no connection to the subject matter
though. One thing that was intriguing was that although
(like most WP games) jumping first down a path has advantages,
there is also some benefit to coming later to particular 
strategies - trains and factories improve over time, so
the first person obtaining them gets less valuable goods.

Solid seeming gameplay with a lot of options, fitting the
modern pattern of difficult to figure out optimal strategy
within the constraints of others' picks. Probably take
anyone a few plays to really grasp.

RATING: 1
Do you want to play a rail game where you get to build sprawling track in Russia and convey goods and passengers over vast distances? Do you want to play a game where you ruthlessly run rail companies, wheel and deal shares on a dynamic stock market, smell the diesel and taste the vodka? If you do, then DO NOT play this game. Have you ever wanted to run an excel spreadsheet on a player board-fruit machine to maximise points on various dials? Well then, this is your game - a dry multiplayer solitaire Euro masquerading as a rail game. The sad part is that there is a generation of gamers out there now who will never have tried the 18xx series. Sure, they have flaws, but I would tell anyone who wants to play a train game to go play one of these ( 1830 ideally to start off ). There you actually build track, buy/sell shares, run a company, engage with players and perform railroad activities that fit in with the theme. It is a sorry state of affairs where a lifeless,  abstract VP fruitsalad like this, without any real railroad features can make the top 50 and get lapped up.

RATING: 7
Choo choooooo....

It's nice to see a fresh take at a train game.  I really enjoy the combination of worker placement and the way the track types work in this game.   

The only problem I've got really is that it appears there are only a small number of viable strategies, and it is absolutely critical that you not do something that someone else is doing in order to maximize your opportunity.   The problem, of course, is that a player who decides to do what you're doing well after you have started doing it can... derail... your game pretty quickly.

So I wish there were more viable strategies, but apart from that this is a great game.

RATING: 8
Rating may be premature, but wow, what an amazing game. It has all of the limiting/blocking tension of a typical worker placement game, but with the most kick-ass action and scoring combos I've ever seen in a Euro. What I love most is that everything in the game seems good: all the tracks are good, the orange tokens are all amazingly good, the engineers are all good, and it is difficult to figure out which combinations of good things will benefit you the most. Building too much infrastructure will make you fall behind over the early rounds, but if you can time it right, you will be scoring an unbelievable number of points by the end of the game (my favorite moment of our first play was when we scored a round and someone said, "WHAT!?! How could you have possibly scored THAT??").

I've never had so much fun doing multiplication.

RATING: 6
A solid worker placement game with multiple paths to victory.  It's pretty fun picking your chosen path and trying to run with it.

However, I think it has a bit of mechanism overload, and isn't quite as streamlined as I like to see.  Some things are a bit clunky feeling, and feel like they may have been added on for the sake of balance.  Either that, or some designers just keep designing well past the point where it seems like a finished game.

I'm not totally saying the complexity and mechanisms and top of mechanisms is bad, per se, especially once you know the game.  It just makes it one of those games I'm totally going to forget how to play between playings, meaning at some point it won't come off the shelf because I won't feel like re-learning it.

Overall, a well done worker placement game on the heavier end of the spectrum; it's just one I don't find myself getting back to or desiring to play.

RATING: 7
Solid worker placement game about building railroads across (you guessed it) Russia! Despite the common train theme, the game is a lot of fun to play and provides ample opportunities to score satisfying combos and score tons of tons of points. My biggest complaint about the game is the absence of variability in the set up. The same actions, goals, and bonuses are in every game and therefore it becomes a bit stale once players have had a chance to figure out the best strategies. Because of this, I tend to prefer its card-based twin, First Class.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This is straight forward individual player board worker placement. Like most games where players have individual boards, this game has a solitaire, puzzle-optimization feel to it. It also has the point salad feel of a Stefan Feld game, but without any of the ingenious mechanisms that make some of his games at least somewhat fun and interesting for me. 

Basically, all you do in this game is build track. Most of the available actions are building track. You can also take more guys/coins to build more track. You can select an engineer character which gives you a private action for the rest of the game - most of the engineer actions are building track. You can take a locomotive action, so you can use your built track for special privileges (extra workers and other bonuses). The purpose of building track is to get different colored track so you can build that track, for even more track-building excitement!!!! And then there is industry, which lets you move a purple disc on another track for points. What variety!

It really is that tedious and boring and repetitive. Even for people that like this type of game, I can't see it having any replayability - the setup is the same each time (outside of having different engineers) and there is little to nothing dynamic happening between the players that would give rise to variability. 

There is nothing in the game that makes you feel like you are doing anything other that moving track shaped pieces in a line. Compare this to Snowdonia, a similarly themed game that I much prefer. There is a great feel of building a shared railroad, clearing rubble and laying track through different weather conditions, until you reach the top of the mountain. Everyone is fighting on the same track, and the weather and order of the dummy actions all add to a very tight and variable experience. 

I give this a few points because the rules are pretty simple - you are up and running relatively quickly and it plays fast. 1st play 4 players was less than 2 hours with rules explanation, so this should come in at 60 minutes with experience. So for those reasons, I wouldn't fight too hard if it were to be suggested by others. But I still can't believe the hype this is getting. Feels very generic to me. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Russian Railroads is a very good game, in fact it’s almost a great game! It’s a very good worker placement game in which you actually aren’t collecting resources and converting them to victory points, so that’s refreshing. It’s relatively deep and has some very interesting strategic decisions. I like that you can pursue various strategies (points from industries, bonus chits, high point rail tracks, doublers on the Sibirian track etc.) Also, there isn’t any randomness in RR at all, except for the starting player and which engineers are available throughout the game; just like in Terra Mystica. This is something I love in hardcore eurogames. Even though some claim that a game need randomness and variety in order to be interesting, Terra Mystica (and perhaps RR) are examples that prove this wrong in my opinion. As said, the only randomness is which engineers are available during the game, but they are all face up before the game starts, so you can build your strategy around those available. 

However… Russian Railroads definitely has a problem. A serious problem actually. And that’s the replayability, or rather lack of it. Sure, there are various engineers, but they don’t really change the game too much, and basically there are only 3 tracks with 3 different strategies (or a combination of them). It’s not just me who raise this question about the lack of replayability – it’s something everybody I know who has played RR also says… On the other hand, I have tried it 13 times, which is an extremely impressive number for me as I often play a game just a couple of times. This is a testament to how great RR is. But there is absolutely no doubt about it that the enjoyment you get out of Russian Railroad diminishes over time as the game starts to feel kind of the same due to how few strategies you can pursue in the game… Regarding these strategies, the two major are probably Vladivostik and Kiev+factories. The St. Petersburg-strategy (double points on track number 7) is a tough one to pull off because it also requires you to advance on the Vladivostok route, and the tracks can net you a lot more points on that route due to the white tracks. However, I can confirm that I’ve won a game by using this strategy, so it’s not impossible, but it’s definitely a much weaker strategy than the rest. 

Oh, and speaking of the St. Petersburg line, I’ve never seen anybody not getting both of their bonus tokens in play (although it was a close call once, and maybe it did happen in 1 out of 13 plays). It’s simply rather easy to get, so it doesn’t make sense not to get those bonuses; and that’s a pity. Again, some more strategies/paths to victory or other action spaces or something would enhance the variety and therefore replayability, because as it stands now there isn’t a whole lot to do. You can mix some paths, but it likely won’t do you any good. RR almost forces you to focus on a single strategy at the beginning of the game and then pound that, whether being Vladivostok or Kiev+factories etc. It’s too hard to change strategy and shift your focus, and I don’t really like the huge focus on strategy over tactics.

Speaking of strategy/tactics, coins seem pretty important as they essentially give you an extra action, though it also means that you forfeit a better action immediately (as you take the coin action now). Workers are also good. But probably the best spot is the engineer, which costs you a gold, so you need to have a coin and be first in player order. It’s not always the case, but it’s honestly quite rare that the starting player doesn’t grab an engineer or coins if he hasn’t got any. The engineers give a bonus action only you can use (so they cannot be blocked), and they’re usually quite powerful. Not only that, but they also give you 40 or 20 points at the end of the game if you have the majority. Speaking of points, you get a gazillion of them in this game! However, unlike Stefan Feld games, you don’t get them for doing everything, and as I said you cannot do a little bit of everything (unlike, say, CoB or Trajan). Winning scores are usually over 400, but it could also very well be below 300 – it depends on the number of players, the engineers and how fast players buy trains; if all of the players sit and wait for the rest to buy the lower numbered trains, the scores will be lower. I’ve actually won every single one of my plays of RR with various strategies (all of them I would say?), and though there isn’t a recipe for victory, I would say that extra coins are important as well as getting an extra worker asap as well as getting at least 3 chips, one of them being the one which gives you an end-game scoring card. Engineers are important too, but I’ve won many games in which I only had 1 or max 2. Also, while the 9-train is powerful, it isn’t broken as some people say.

I’ve played it a handful of times as a two player game – and those games have taken around 30-45 minutes with one of the games taking 20 minutes! Yes, 20 minutes! That’s just insane! And it wasn’t because we didn’t think about what to do, it’s just that if you have a strategy, you know what to do without thinking too much. One of my plays with 3 players took 1 hour, and for such a relatively meaty game this is awesome! It’s a tense game with 4; with 2, the competition isn’t as fierce as there are too many spots (as is the case with 3), and you probably go for two different strategies meaning you don’t really compete over the same spots. But, truth be told, I would play this game again with any number of players!

So, all in all I like Russian Railroads, but I have to once again mention the caveat of the limited replayability. I played it 8 times in 1 month and 12 times from November to January, but I haven’t played it since. I think RR benefits of not being played repeatedly because of the few strategies available – and that’s why I ultimately ended up selling it. Still, it’s a good game and I hope they publish an expansion which opens up for more strategies, more variety in the setup and hence a better replayability. Edit: they have released an expansion, German Railroads... So now I had to purchase RR again, and I don't regret it one bit as it really is an excellent game, especially if not played extensively in a short span (but that goes for almost every game except for perhaps Agricola and games like that).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wonderfully deep worker placement game.  (Despite the 'rail' name/theme, it is not a 'rail' game).  You place workers on the main game board to build several increasingly valuable tracks along three routes, one main route and two smaller ones.  The main track will likely be the lion's share of your points, while the smaller tracks offer bonuses to help you win.  There is also an industrial track that mechanically works very similar to the above tracks, which offer custom bonuses based on the type of factory you build there.  Engineers can be employed and eventually purchased to assist in your domination of the Russian rail system. 

It's a wonderfully designed game in almost every way.  The only possible negative is that it is very math intensive.  Scoring happens seven times during the game and final scores can reach several hundred points.  While this isn't an issue for my group, others may be turned off on it.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Too many notes, my dear Mozart.

A toybox giddily overstuffed with bits and gewgaws and ever more ways to score points. Plays like De Vulgari Eloquentia if someone swapped out your Adderall for MDMA, a la the old commercials for Folgers Crystals. It's a solidly designed game at heart and I'm sure it would reward repeated play but like a lot of the currently chic style of worker placement games with their nominal-at-best themes and their crying lack of interaction and their propensity to Make It Rain with the VPs like so many NFL defensive backs in so many seedy gentlemen's clubs, I ultimately found myself indifferent.

Slap these same mechanics on a game with a narrative that justifies them and I might come around for a second helping.

RATING: 7
Initial reaction after one game:
After reading the rules, this felt like an easy 8+. But upon playing it, I realized that I don't think it has enough variety for me. I mean, there are different paths you can take, but there isn't much interacting with other players along the way. I mean, yeah, you're competing for the same action spaces, those action spaces make up like 97% of the player interaction. This is pretty much a multi-player solitaire optimization exercise. Honestly I don't think players even really cared what other players were doing. Even when scoring (simultaneously), all players added up their own scores, and moved their score markers. 

I would really like to see a little more game-induced variety. There is a [i]tiny[/i] bit in the order of engineers that come out. Which is seen during setup, and zero randomness during the game. Engineers are kinda nice in that they are worker spaces that are not static. But that's not much. Maybe everyone should be dealt a random end-game goal card or something--I don't know--but this definitely feels like the kind of game where a player can decide they're going to do XYZ and then proceed to do exactly that. I'm realizing I'm not a big fan of games where players can map out the whole game in advance.

Having said all that, it's still a decent worker placement game, which I hope to get a few more plays out of.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
10 years of worker placement design has culminated in this offspring: stock-standard worker placement choices of Snowdonian simplicity. Nothing original has been attempted here, and that is to some extent saddening: more was definitely possible here. Of course there is some blocking of desired actions, but if that is an issue move the starting player through an action and do what you want on the next turn.

The game itself is then nearly completely relegated to the players' own boards. In the finest Euro tradition these have nothing to do with the supposed subject, and are all about whirls and whorls to squeeze points from their structures. Differently coloured H-profile tokens walk down track, with later pieces never being able to overtake earlier ones. Since points are to be had for how much track is behind a coloured piece, you want the highest grossing colour to be as far along the track as possible, necessitating movement of all preceding colours even further. Along the track there are various events like H-profile colour unlocking, bonus points, bonus tokens, and world peace. In total there are four tracks: three resembling a railroad where the profile tokens move, and one for a simple hexagon token which simply moves from start to end representing level of industrial development. The further long a player is along this track, the more points he obtains. To make matters even more difficult a 'train' indicates the maximum distance to which points are awarded: so while your profile tokens can be further along the track, if the train doesn't match them, you've been inefficient. Trains can be upgraded, and the upgraded trains turn over and become one time industry actions which fill in gaps in the industry track.

The game plays more or less as you might imagine. It is a point amassing design which is mostly uninteresting: and it is that because it really adds nothing to the body of titles already out there. But I suppose one can say it works, and that is not altogether bad praise. What I disliked after my first skirmishes though, is that the dynamics of VP gathering are far in my uncomfortable zone, to the point of unplayability: with a little effort scores of 400+ points are possible. Considering that with the same effort you can score 125+ of them on the last of your 7 turns, and that you start out with 15- on your first turn, this means that the range of VP handed out spans two and a half magnitudes (!). Small errors in the beginning can be tremendously blown up: not a pattern I enjoy. But it will probably appeal to those who want to have their games go out with a Bang.

All in all a playable game, but not one I'm keen on revisiting any time soon. It would have gotten a 5 were it not for the uninspiring worker placement core, the weird scoring dynamics, and the atrocious rulebook (which I haven't even mentioned yet).

[b]Update[/b] Fellow Geek Paul Lister summed it up [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/listitem/3034987?commentid=4376563#comment4376563]thusly[/url]: [i]Gamer cat nip — watch them roll over and start dribbling at all the buttons to push, and three victory point scratching posts![/i] Exactly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.75
I've now played RR online enough that I feel comfortable rating it, and in fact I enjoyed it enough to buy it!

This is one of those games that could be perceived as multi-player solitaire, because you're trying to pick a strategy to focus on, and continually do what you can to get to that end.  The main competition is in the action spaces for your workers, which can get a bit more cutthroat as the game progresses.

What I like about this is the myriad scoring opportunities.  There's a lot to explore here, and repeated plays mean you'll likely be able to perfect your strategies.  It moves very quickly once you get the hang of it, and I can see my friends getting very absorbed in this interesting game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The workers placement mechanics seem completely copied from Lords of Waterdeep. On top of this the game add an extra strategic layer with the individual players board developments.

The result is a game less streamlined and thematic than LoW, but more complex/deep.

I really liked my first game (2 players) but the more I played it the more I had issues with the games:
- Victory points score exponentially as turns move on. this is not necessary a bad thing but here it is so extreme, that you can score more VP in the 7th and last turn than in all previous turns.
- This encourage choosing one strategy early on and sticking to it.
- Once each players have choose their "routes" to victory points. The workers placement core mechanisms, may result in more or less contested choices just based on those strategies.
- If you try to take another players desired action you'll loose a lot of potential point, compared to maximizing your own score. This is OK in a 2 players game, but with more, the others players will benefit more from you attempt to block someone than you.
- For a workers placement game, I felt that some choices were so automatic it almost reduced the decision to zero (same patterns appeared every turns) in the early placement rounds each turns while the final placement where not contested at all.
- Workers placement with limited game turns (7) is not so great.
- Some special actions(extra black worker) or engineers are very powerful and might affect balance.


Overall I had the feeling to be on "rails" from start to finish despite tactical decisions making.

RATING: 9
Point salad, right up my alley! Found this on Yucata.de, though I'm fairly confident this will end up being a purchase before long. The railroad theme is quite thin; the rules and mechanics may as well be applied to cupcake making, but the options for point gathering are well-balanced, and everyone has the same options for play since everyone has their own tracks.

Interaction comes via the worker placement mechanic, which may disturb your decision tree as you would expect in a WP game. So I don't feel like I'm playing multi-player solitaire, but at the same time I don't feel like other people's plays are fully dictating my strategy, forcing me onto a specific track (see what I did there?)

If I had one thing to change, it would be to add the ability to use an opponent's purchased Engineers, at a cost of one coin.

RATING: 7
[2015-02-12 Rating: n.a. -> 8]
While Russian Railroads (RR) doesn't do anything groundbreaking new and is at it's heart a worker placement game coupled with engine building, I do really like it. Although I'm normally not in the running for the win, I have a lot of fun optimizing my own play and discovering new tricks. I find it very satisfying to choose a strategy at the beginning of a game and trying to execute it as good as I can. The only downside I can see is that people could tend to choose the same strategy that worked for them over and over again, thus reducing the replayability a bit since plays could feel samey. But that isn't the failure of the game and I've yet to get to this point.

Maybe I'll change my rating at a later point in time, but until then Russian Railroads gets two thumbs up.

RATING: 8
Players are Russian railroad moguls, trying to milk the most victory points from the game as they develop three railroads, and the associated industry.

Standard worker placement fare, but from the wonderful publisher Hans im Glueck, so this one is worth a look.  We are at the meaty end of the spectrum here, as players acquire rail, trains and industry advancements to unlock bonuses on their private player board.  The key is to get a victory point generating engine up and running over the six or so turns in the game, and not be left behind.  Scores are totalled at the end of each round and added to the tally, and begin to escalate at an alarming rate.  

Lovely design - thinky, without overdoing it.  The presentation of the game, rules and components, is striking and first class.  No fluff here - no resources to collect and mince into other resources, no workers to feed - just collect the points.  Refreshing.

Excellent game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

This is one of the best games of 2013 and should see tons of play in 2014.
Highly recommended.

RATING: 7
In the same way that Dominant Species taught me the ways that worker placement games can be amazing and unique, Russian Railroads taught me the ways that a worker placement came can be look amazing from the outset but ultimately be pretty standard.

While the points-game of Russian Railroads is where the buzz is at (clever play will exponentially explode your score gain each turn,) the actions just come across as yet-another-worker-placement-game. Certainly not a bad thing, but not worthy of massive praise either.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A solid worker-placement game but compared to similar games, there are a few issues I found.  

Like the German educational system, you basically have to pick your career path/track rather early on in the game before you really know what you are doing or what or want to do and stick to it right until the end; changing a strategy halfway through isn’t really an option.  

It is generally to the benefit of each player to take a different track/path so that they are not competing over resources.  This takes away from the very tension of most worker-placement games of trying to anticipate/bluff with other players to see who will snap up much needed resources first.  Basically, you pick a unique track/path and continuously place workers on the same spaces round after round to continuously to obtain all the resources you need and keep doing it until the end of the game with the hope that your path is more successful than anyone else’s path.  Thus, the game feels very scripted.  Some paths also do seem more convoluted than others, but apparently the designers of the game do say each track/path is a viable one to victory.  

Something that I'm not a fan of is that you can actively look though the special action cards and select the one that you want.  This stops the game dead-in-its-track (as players read through the rule book and debate which one to take), experienced players automatically know which cards to get which brings me to my third point: some special actions seem overpowered compared to others and feel unbalanced in the game.  This seems apparent in the run-away leader problems here as scores between players vary much more widely here than in similar worker placement games.  

Many people complain about Euro-games being dry, soulless and without theme; however while I almost always disagree with that statement (good mechanics in a game are always better than theme), "Russian Railroads" really is the poster-child for that criticism.  You basically take a spot so you can move a black cube to the right, so that you can later take a spot to move a brown cube, then a white cube, and that's basically it.  At no point do I feel this game has anything to do with railroads or building a railroad company.  And as for interaction: while I'm not a big proponent for mandated-interaction in games, this is one of the few games where we really all sat in complete silence as taking spots doesn't really interfere much with other players (see 'everyone takes their own unique path' statement above).  

While it is a solid and well thought-out game mechanically, I would recommend games such as Pillars of the Earth, Lords of Waterdeep, Lancaster, Agricola/Caverna over this one as they are more balanced, streamlined, interactive, thematic and engaging.   

As a lark, they should re-theme this game with Gymnasium, Realschule, and Hauptschule tracks.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
[b][FirstImpression22ndNOV2013][/b]One of the best crop of Spiel 2013. Played this a month after it's release (Spiel 2013) and I was spot on about me liking this game.

I like how pushing the rail works on your player board, and the use of trains (that has values on them) reminds me so much about 18xx (which is no surprise as the game was designed by 18xx fellas). The factory marker that serves as a bridge for your industry marker also pretty neat (there's a grove at the bottom of your player board which you can slide in a flipped train). All in all, in terms of function and design, they all worked perfectly.

In terms of gameplay, it's just a typical worker placement game where you send your worker(s) on the board and do the action immediately. Nothing so great about the part. But the decision and the action that you do onto your player board, to see how it all function and work are probably my favorite part. There are a lot to focus on, and choosing to focus in a certain area is probably the best choice.

In our first 4 players game, it took us nearly 2 hours, and that didn't include the explanation. The first 1-2 rounds of the game, players were still figuring out the available actions, 3-6 where it got more serious therefore were the most AP prone rounds, and the last round went very quickly as everyone knew what to do to maximize their points. 

I still think the Engineers are powerful and it is everyone's job to compete to get the engineers. Otherwise, 1-2 players will have too many engineers on their side and that will benefit them greatly (although the values on the engineers actually balance that out).

The point scoring in the game is nuts as they increase in folds, round after round. If your total points in a round is about the same as your previous round, then you know you're not doing so well. 

This is again, one of my favorite game from Spiel 2013. Believe the hype.

[b]Update[/b] Sold the game. After repeated plays, I've found a strategy that I like and proved to be a winning strategy for me against my opponent. My subsequent plays, and my stubbornness to experiment with new strategy proved to be an experience of boredom for myself. 

[b]Status: Purchased on MARCH 1st 2014[/b]
[b]Status: Sold, September 2015[/b]

RATING: 8
I love it when you hear claims that a game is broken and that there is just one path to Victory, and then when you dig deeper you find that different people are claiming a different path. So don't believe it when someone tells you that Russian Railroads is a broken game. It is not. This is a real thinky worker placement game. If you are in the market for a more complex Stone Age, of perhaps near Tzolk'in complexity, then this could be the game for you. There is lots going on and it all moves along seamlessly with four distinct tracks available to gather rails or technologies for. I am definitely getting the impression that specialising in one is the way to go. Which is a shame as it is good to find a game where doing a bit of everything can reap as strong a reward as overt specialisation. Still, whilst not especially innovative, this is a good, solid, and worthwhile addition to my games collection. Perhaps my only reservation is that the last round is definitely one of points optimisation and so can slow down as AP sets in and people work out the best use of the final turn.

RATING: 7
This is a good worker placement game that doesn't involve feeding your people. You are developing on 3 rail tracks and an industry track. Every game, you will have a different assortment of Engineers and there are various bonus cards and tiles that you can pursue based on your strategy. One thing I dislike is that it seems easy for someone to miscalculate their scores each round. You can slow the game down by everyone showing how they calculated their points so less mistakes happen, or everyone doing their own and not only risk mistakes, but not really understanding where they are getting their points. This is less of an issue when playing via yucata.de (an awesome digital implementation). I worry the various tracks are not evenly balanced which could lead to replay value issues, but the expansion addresses this issue. After the initial setup, there is virtually no luck in this game, which some will find appealing.

RATING: 8
This is one giant bottleneck puzzle. Identifying these bottlenecks in advance and capitalizing on those of your opponents is crucial. There is a constant struggle to keep the low scoring tracks pushing forward, to accommodate the higher scoring tracks, but without pushing your other tracks forward to capitalize on points you'll get left in the dust. The round end scoring is tedious, but it forces players to develop both long and short term strategies, so the game is less about high return on investment as it is about making several micro improvements every turn. The base game seems to be solvable, at least to a certain extent, but the German Railroads expansion blows that concern to smithereens. Also the length is perfectly calibrated for this game, and magnifies everything this game has going for it.

RATING: 7
Point Salad euro. Good for what it is, it doesn't re-invent the genre so it won't change your mind about this kind of game if you don't like them already. Pretty standard worker placement to build your point-producing engine that just gets better and better each round. There are no catch up mechanisms, no punishment mechanisms. You just snowball your way to victory. There is no theme. You never feel like you're in Russia or building a railroad. Player interaction is minimal, the standard action denial of most worker placement games. There are a lot of components in this game which can be fiddly to set up and put away, similar to other euros like Puerto Rico or Castles of Burgundy. Component quality, graphic design, and art are all excellent. The decisions are engaging. The game moves pretty fast for it's weight. There are a lot of icons to learn about but they become intuitive quickly. Overall, a good heavier euro.

RATING: 6
I'm glad I had the opportunity to play Russian Railroads, as the reviews I had read had sparked my interest. In practice, I found it to be a very workman-like worker placement game with a pasted-on railroad theme, wherein the primary form of competition is the standard limited number of spaces for workers (with the same three or four such spaces being claimed immediately each round). There are multiple tracks on which to push cubes (or collect tokens) to score points, but focusing on one or two leads to compounding (if not exponential) victory points, as long as the related actions don't see too much competition from other players. All in all, Russian Railroads feels very much like multiplayer solitaire/spreadsheets and not at all like a railroad game. It's a competent (and relatively heavy) worker placement game that I'd be willing to play again, but have no desire to own or recommend.

RATING: 8
(Rating based on 110+ asynch online plays on Yucata.de [microbadge=19345])

The board game equivalent to a pinball table. You get points and bonuses for everything - and then some more. There's almost no theme, but the mechanics are intriguing enough on their own to carry this game.

It rewards multiple plays so if you play it for your first time, chances are you will get slaughtered. You need to at least know the basic strategies so you know what to do.

My favorite part of this game is that you have the ability to pull off some huge combos and monster turns. Especially when everybody has passed early and you can take multiple turns in a row.

The only downside is that the game can feel a bit samey when you've played it a bunch of times because there's not much variation in the setup. You have different engineers, but that's about it.

RATING: 6
This is what one geekbud calls a "churn-and-burn" game in that you get through the arc of discovery and (relative) mastery pretty quickly. I see the appeal of trying to beat your high score while navigating the tight worker placement spaces the game presents, but after one play I can already see the end of the ride. 

The game is basically a dressed up spreadsheet where you claim cells before other players do so you can add up higher and higher amounts each turn. Each turn, more spreadsheet cells are up for competition. Any relation to railroads is strictly incidental. People who like heavy doses of incrementalism and positive feedback will be in heaven here; be wary of their ratings & comments unless you like that too.

I'll probably play again at the request of my group because I found the worker placement interesting. Among equally experienced players perhaps the competition for the 9-train and scoring in general makes for a compelling  experience. I can't help but feel like I've seen all there is after one game so it remains to be seen if trying to achieve higher and higher scores will hold my interest. I could see the game going up a point, but for now, I came in 2nd my first game at 344 and that feels like enough for me.

RATING: 8
Fab'
October 2013
1x 4-players
1x 3-players

Played twice : once at the Spiel'13 game fair and once at home, so rating might change in the future. Big up for the guys at the Z-man Games boot btw, for providing good explanation of this somewhat complex game, in the context of the Spiel, is somehing of an achievement. Really enjoyed both games, and want to play more. A first remark : there is absolutely no theme in this baroque worker-placement game, despite the great cover and the nice graphics. It is quite difficult to actually understand what on earth you might be doing by moving black, then grey, then brown, then natural, then white track pieces... on preexisting tracks (???). Nevertheless, the game is tense, very tense, without any downtime, every move is important. Some obvious strategic axes are detected by players in the first rounds (if not during the rules explanation), and all seem to work rather nicely. For instance, I won a game by maxim out my transsiberian and ignoring industries, and the other one by developing industries and maxing out the two "short" railways. Further, the game is nicely produced, with great components, in with beauty does not obfuscate function -a very important point in my eyes-. Further, while long, the rules are extremely clear.

From my inexperienced point of view, a few points might bother me in the long term :
- the game seem overly, or to be frank, artificially complicated. [Update: Does not really bug me after 2 games].
-I feel that some obligatory steps have to be taken by all players (maybe getting an extra worker and getting cards at least). [Update: getting last round scoring cards seems difficult to ignore. In our last game, I scored more than 60 pts -two end cards- in a game that was decided in 370 points, so 1/6 of my score came from the card and 5/6 from the 6 scoring phases (n.b: 3-player games last only 6 rounds)]. 
-I wonder how the number of players affects the overall balance of the game. [Update: 3-player games are just as tense as 4-player one. Still have to try the 2-players configuration]

RATING: 10
Purest worker placement! A lot of strategy, planning and game vision are crucial to victory in this game with luck factor absent in game mechanics. We will build our railroads by allocating our workers in the basic actions available on the main board and in engineers who bring special actions. The development of the railways will release improvements and bonuses for the players. The game lasts several rounds, at the end of each one a score is made. In the first rounds there are few points and at the end of the last one, with the sum of the bonus cards, it is possible to double the score. A game with elastic scores in which a win by 20 points has been won in one detail and a big win can only be considered with more than 80 points difference! *** Best with 3 players ***

RATING: 5.5
With 2 players, while it looks like there are many viable paths to victory, the reality is quite different: the game is good at obfuscating which choices are best, but after a while you'll be able to evaluate every strategy's different growth curve and determine which is the best one. Done that, the game will become a battle of optimization between the two players, where all but one strategy are discarded. Or at least that's what I'm seeing. A possible way to fix it would be to find a better spot for the extra worker in the Vladivostok line, at the moment I believe it helps too much the strategy that is already giving the best returns.

For 3+ players the game might be better if the increased competition makes the not-too-good strategies viable.

RATING: 8.5
Rules Complexity: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:
Strategic Depth: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Luck Factor :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Negotiation Factor: :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime: :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness: :star::halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Replayability: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Component Quality: :star::star::star::star::star:
Player Interaction: :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :halfstar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Theme Integration: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:

A very solid, elegantly produced, tight worker placement game about building the Trans-Siberian railroad.  This is a game where you will only be able to do about half of what you'd like to as you move along up to 4 different progress "tracks" simultaneously. It feels a little more scripted than I was expecting, but the wealth of options that enable you to progress keeps it feeling tense and engaging. My only real con is the amount of math necessary to add up scores at the end of each round.  You start out getting a few points after the first round, but by the last you may be getting well over a hundred.  Don't let the somewhat pasted-on train theme scare you: if you like worker placement, you'll probably love this!

RATING: 6
Worker placement and engine building go together fairly often but rarely is the snowball effect more pronounced than in Russian Railroads.

Mechanically, the game offers nothing new, you've seen it all before. The execution is top-notch, though, straight-foward in both gameplay and design. It's a crowded genre but if you're still looking for a worker placement game and don't have an aversion to either trains or snowballs you can do a lot worse.

You should be aware, however, that you may feel a little railroaded by the game after a few runs. The game offers a few very clear-cut tracks to run on, and the challenge is solely in figuring out how to squeeze another point out of the available actions. Russian Railroads doesn't tolerate dithering or switching strategies mid-game. You've got to choose one lever and crank it hard. While that's okay for the first couple of plays it certainly becomes a little same-y after a while.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
First multi-player game was really great. Took two hours from the start of the game. All three of us really, really liked it. It isn't so much a brain-burner, but it does take some time to think of your choice, especially when the persons before you take what you had planned to do. 

There are so many things you can do on a turn - build track (one or two, different colors); build a locomotive or a factory; advance on the industrial track; take a doubler, or take money, or take two temp workers; or do an engineers action, buy an engineer; or move up on the Turn order track. 

You will have from 5-8 or 10 actions you may take per turn depending on how many workers and how much money you have. 

There are tracks that give you bonuses as you advance along them. Their are locomotives that provide points based on where your track is laid. There are engineers that that give you special benefits. There are Factories you can build that can score points, or special bonuses. There are End Game Scoring Bonuses you can secretly obtain. The list goes on. So much happening.

Yet, the game flows so intuitively. The hardest part to grasp is how to score. 

A great, great game! I really like it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Russian Railroads starts off in the stratosphere: excellent rulebook, well-designed components and graphic design, and a scoring mechanism (involving moving pieces of "track") that I haven't seen anywhere else and I found highly interesting.  I was very much thinking this an '8' game after the first two rounds.  But by half the game, it begins to feel repetitive, the mini-maxing increases to a point that becomes irritating, and the runaway leader problem starts to rear its ugly head - and there's still half of a game to go!  I definitely liked it all the way through, but by the last couple rounds I was sort of ready for it to end.  Rarely have my expectations built so highly at the start of a game and become rather deflated by the end.  Still, I rate this game a '7' for its interesting scoring mechanic - while though mathy and abstracted from the theme - is unique and should be noted.  But there is definitely a feeling of the game running away from itself by the end.  The compounding score "thing" feels a little ridiculous and I wish there was some mechanic which would have curbed it. So- good game, but not consistently intriguing to really make it stand out from all the other excellent worker placement games out there.

RATING: 10
Excellent worker placement strategic game. Great theme, excellent components and simple, yet very deep game play. In Russian Railroads there are many ways to score victory points together game engine building and tough worker placement decisions, which all make this game one of the best worker placement games I have played. Heavy competition for all good places to place workers. Engineers are great, but not overpowered. Everything seems to be so smooth and balanced in this game. Scoring is mathy in way that many different points are accumulating, but that makes this game great, because you can always find interesting way to score points.
Theme exists in game, however thematically this game is made from railway building manager desk perspective. This game is not about building railway actually as you see it, but as an manager you hire engineers, plan and give orders.

RATING: 10
I love this game, so many options but the victory point paths are highly transparent. That is, there's no esoteric convert resource X->Y->Z->points as in Rosenberg games (which I love) so strategy becomes more important. The game is indirectly interactive in the way worker placement games tend to be, but here the number of "good" actions is highly limited so it's more intense. 

Probably a game I will keep in my "always play" stack with Castles, Puerto Rico, Ora, etc.

UPDATE: After 7 plays, I still love this game and always want to play it! I'm seeing some of the more strategic points (not every engineer is worth hiring even for the 40 point bonus, for example, and countering at least one opponent's strategy is important). Fantastic game.

RATING: 3.5
The core mechanic is bland worker placement, wrapped in a ridiculously convoluted scoring system - which is triggered at the end of every round. You can move 3 to 5 markers with different values in each of 3 different tracks, build factories in yet another track, get locomotives and hire engineers. If you reach certain points in your tracks with a specific marker, you can unlock other markers, gain new workers, get bonus points or choose among 7 bonus chips with varied effects. And all of this is completely abstract.

I admit the game works. There are various strategic paths and no randomness after setup. But having played it just once, I have no idea if it's balanced, and considering how dry the experience was, it's unlikely that I will ever find out. It's simply one of those spreadsheet efficiency exercises without any semblance of fun.

RATING: 7
Given, this is a good game, but it's just the same old stuff. Place workers, block others and make points in xy different ways. This is really the opposite of innovation. I do miss interaction, as it's too easy to just play your game in a very straight way.

After more plays, it's still fun to plan ahead and all, but it lacks interaction that is needed to force the players to some more tactical moves. As it is now, you can simply go your way, no matter what.

Using industry is the most fun you can get in this game, as there are different possibilities, while all other strategys are set in stone. Not to mention that there are only a few viable strategys available, which is bad for the replayability.

Also, Saint Petersburg is by far the worst track. Yes, you can get 2 very useful and strong "?" markers there, but that's all the track has to offer. They could have ended the track right there, as it's a waste of time to spend anything else there. This gives the game a bad aftertaste.

More plays confirmed my guess about the replayability. There are only 3 strategys and one of them does "always" grand you the 1st or 2nd place.

RATING: 4
While there is nothing that is new here, this game does what it does relatively well. And what is it that this game does? Carefully blends the euro-tropes of new (e.g., worker placement) and old (e.g., resource conversion) into something that still feels relatively fresh. While the lack of actual train connections (say on a map) makes the link to the theme a bit tenuous at best, the game still provides an interesting mechanical structure within which to compete with other "railroad" barons. This game, like many of its brethren, encourages specialization (through and with an economic snowball) -- however, with only one play, I am curious as to whether the multiple paths to victory are relatively similarly weighted or if they are supposed to  balance out through competition for positions.
Overall, good but not great.

RATING: N/A
40
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
(05/11/2014) Comment based on 4 plays.

An interesting twist on worker placement, with a somewhat well integrated railroad theme. However, it is not a route network building game.

On my first play, this seemed like "Just Another Soulless Euro" worker placement. Each player gets five/six workers, just like in Stone Age, and there are a myriad of action spaces that are on a first-come, first-served basis. There seemed to be clearly two obvious paths to victory. Either: 1) Build the Trans-Siberian Railway (the top track), or 2) Build a bunch of industry (along the bottom track). However, whoever can leverage one of the two middle tracks along with one of these strategies will likely win.

Re-playability seemed questionable at first, since there are only two likely paths to victory. However, it seems that the character of every game will be determined by the random assortment of engineers that will come out each game. There is also the hidden element of some end-of-game victory cards not being available. Still, after four plays, the game starts to feel same-y.

It is unlikely that a player will win with a "balanced" approach, making progress along all four tracks, because it is simply not possible, given the limited action spaces and limited turns. Thus, there seems to be issues with player count. With three, two players will go for one of the two paths, while leaving the third player free to maximize and win with the other path. With four, two will go for the top, while the other two will go for the bottom. At that point, it is a matter of the player that best strings together actions and benefits together to maximize play. 

The theme drips with pre-Soviet Russia, yet there really is no feeling of building a railroad or network. There is the innovative mechanism of having five different "rail tokens" inch-worming across the top track, as it is a race to see if one can build from Moscow to Vladivostok, but beyond that, it is a matter of grabbing action spaces before others do.

The components are very good, and there are lots and lots of bits, which will make any Euro-fan happy. The rules are very well written, too, with most corner cases covered. Play is smooth and fast, with very little down time. If you want a worker placement, that has a lot of bells and whistles, that forces you to optimize and jump through a lot of hoops, then this Euro is for you. Personally, I don't find this design that interesting. It is a solid "7".

(05/17/2014) Update after 5 plays. Someone in our group finally broke 400 VP! It is possible to complete all three of the upper tracks, and half-way through the bottom industry track. Groupthink thought this was impossible--either chose the upper or lower track, with some leveraging in the middle two tracks. The lower industry-only strategy garners huge number of VP early on, enough to discourage the players pursuing the top-track strategy, but the top-track strategy eventually catches up, and exceeds the industry-only strategy, with VP ranging from 125-145 VP, compared to 55-85 VP. There's plenty of interesting, non-linear decisions to be made for a 2-hour game. Still not convinced this is a great game; it is a solidly "good" game.

(12/06/2014) Update after 8 plays. The group appeal is wearing off, finally--I'd been quietly putting up with this design for months. I claimed early on that there were only a few paths to victory, but a few people claimed there were still lots of choices to explore, so I reluctantly went along, although I could not articulate it.

Then, BGG User [user=qwertymartin]qwertymartin[/user] summarized it most eloquently here in his [blogpost=36394]blog[/blogpost]:

[i]A game of choosing one of (at best) a handful of baked-in paths at the outset and spending the rest of the game executing it as efficiently as you possibly can. Player interaction consists of being aware of the furrows the other players are ploughing so that you can identify the 'hot zones' on the shared worker placement board that you need to prioritize.[/i]

I couldn't have said it any better. It is not very satisfying when all you're doing is quickly select, and then build on a chosen path as efficiently as possible.

RATING: 8.21
1.鐵路板塊拿取一律由最低分開始拿取。
2.在3工人領取火車頭跟工業板塊  是一定要一個火車頭+工業版塊
  注意火車頭4個放滿後，多的火車就要炸回供應區，火車頭可以用替換方式但一定要以大數   
  字換小數字。 工業板塊放入後不得更換
3.1號火車頭後面工業能力為加總目前工程師分數
4.錢可以代替工人，若有錢的符號一定要用錢購買。
5.獎勵必須要火車頭到才會有，可以鐵路先蓋候補獎勵
6.獎勵卡可以選擇不領取+10分
7.起始設置，4人遊戲：5個工人、2或3人遊戲：6個工人，每人1元，洗混順位卡分發
>把4張起始獎勵卡攤開，由尾家先選擇並拿取對應的獎勵，然後按順位數字遞減依序選擇，起始玩家不拿取起始獎勵卡。
結束遊戲獎勵卡只有8張。(其餘挑掉)
8.!!!!!最後一回合必須要放多的行動軌蓋住順位格增加行動!!!!!
9.順位選擇
>不可以擺放工人在目前自己工人順位下
>工人放置後，所有玩家pass完畢，先調順位，然後由第二玩家再放置工人
>工人只能擺放一個工人位置，不能用硬幣增加數目

起始五張獎勵卡打開，額外加分卡暗抽掉兩張，火車頭分類放好(2-9)，工程師照AB分類洗勻，三個A放右邊，四個B放左邊
*每個人七個工人(一個放回盒子)，起始五個工人一塊錢可用，個人圖板最上面放一個1的火車頭(其他沒用到的1收回盒中)，黑鐵軌放第一格，工業軌放對應位置，每個人再拿額外工業軌，獎勵圓片
*隨機發順位卡給每個人，在照反順位拿起始獎勵卡，獎勵資源拿了卡片就移走，起始玩家無獎勵
*每回合輪流放工人，放完工人就執行行動，沒事做就pass根據順位卡計分，七輪後結束，每輪結束計分，錢可取代工人
1.推鐵軌 可分開推不同鐵路的同顏色軌，後面顏色不能超過前面的
2.火車頭 拿數字最小的火車頭放或當成工廠(工廠可拿供應區的)，火車頭放的時候取代其他的，被取代的可放到其他格，如果都滿就放回供應區成為工廠，工廠五個蓋滿後可以任意取代
3. 僱工程師 僱完的成為該玩家的額外行動區
4. 順位格 不能踩在自己順位下面，一個人只能採一格，沒踩的往右推，該工人可以在移動到其他格(第二格先動)，如果首位玩家採第二格且第一個沒人踩，順位不換
*個人圖板如果有符合該標記可獲得獎勵(有火車頭的話要火車頭能涵蓋到)
*每輪計分 每條鐵軌案火車頭涵蓋到的範圍計鐵路顏色分數，還有工廠計分
*之後工程師右移，沒被僱的就移出遊戲，最後一輪順位格會被額外行動取代
*遊戲最後工程師額外加分(數量平手就比數字最大)跟額外加分卡加分
*兩人用另一面圖版，每個火車頭用兩個，工程師3A3B，工人全用，起始六個，起始兩塊錢，可踩在自己的順位格上
*三人火車頭用三個，工程師3A3B，最左邊空著，工人全用起始六個

RATING: 7
Worker placement games aren't normally my favourite genre, but I found this game very enjoyable, if lacking the fun theme of Caverna. It's all rather abstract, I can't fathom what the black-grey-brown-natural-white track upgrade sequence represents in thematic terms, but there are plenty of action choices on each turn. Two problems: the first is that there are only 2 viable strategies: Kiev with Medal and Industry. I've won with both, but without the right engineers in the game the Trans-Siberian just isn't competitive. Second, the game's a snowball, you can tell who's in contention after just a couple of rounds. I really want to like this, but it falls into the 'so nearly' camp for me, I love the theme but little balances just aren't there. This is still among the best games from Essen 2013, but in my group it went off the rails after just a few plays.

RATING: 8
Loose thoughts:
- Seems to emphasize forward planning a lot. Having a game plan can really help you out.
- Turn order and roubles interact in an interesting way. Spending your last rouble doesn't necessarily help you, but if someone else is in front of you with cash on hand, it gives them a big leg up to know when they have to take certain actions.
- Focus on figuring out what actions other players cannot get a benefit out of right now. There's no point in taking a non-contested action early if there's a chance you can wheel it back to your next turn.
- Seems kind of like Agricola, in that early games of players tend to look very different, while late games tend to standardize a bit. Not as pronounced, but notable. Will be interesting to see if any wild strategies start to spring up.

Sleeved in Fantasy Flight clears.
