RATING: 7
Based on train theme, Russian RailRoads is combined worker placement as a main mechanic. Unlike the game name of Russian Railroads, the game is not a classic train game, but actually a worker placement game. This game has a lot of advantages to attract gamers. First, the game provides easy-to-understand rules, so that players can enjoy smooth progress of game from the game beginning to the end. Second, the game has successfully shortend long train game such as 18xx games without removing fun and theme. Surely, Russian RailRoads is one of the best games issued in 2013. Though, it is quite sad that Russian RailRoads has not overcome the limit of worker placement system. It has only increased the list of worker placement games. When I assume the game without the train theme, the game is just a normal worker placement game. On the one way, as a worker placement game, Russian Railroads is surely a wonderful game. On the other way, Russian Railroads has remained a sense of frustration while I am considering the game as train games. After couple of plays, I've discovered certain repetitive play patterns by game participants. The first move of the first player is usually settled to the Engineers action. Because the Engineers action provides personal action choice and big scores at the end, the first move is quite limited. I doubt this kind of so-called standardized patterns easily lower the game value.

RATING: 5
I generally enjoy games which others would consider dry and boring.  I can't quite put my finger on it, but I really do not enjoy this game.  I may be overlooking something, but I just do not find the decisions in this game to be interesting.  Moreover, a main part of the game is moving tokens (i.e, track and industry markers) up their respective tracks.  It's simply hard to get excited about any turns in this game.  Woot! I got to the White track space before you and pushed the white track marker two spaces head.  Take that!

Other games have tracks where you essentially push markers ahead on tracks which I do enjoy (e.g., Goa, Endeavor, Hansa Teutonica).  I think the big difference is that in those other games, I get a sense (perhaps wrongly) that I'm accomplishing things other than simply moving markers up their respective tracks.  In other words, there seems to a be a bigger separation between the actions and the tracks.  Though I'll admit that such separation is fairly lacking in Goa.

RATING: 7.5
Probably the most streamlined of any worker placement game I've played in a while. I like how the actions integrate with each other and the tension for getting what you want when you want it.

However, it really needs a map. Railroads need to be contested. While I can see why game-wise that each player is working on the same railroads independently (on each player's own mat), I can't get over the illogical nature.

In our game, too many ways to score led to everyone being at about the same score at game's end. There was some degree of screw-age in the action-taking round there just wasn't enough of it overall. I didn't feel enough "uh oh"s, because usually there were multiple good things I could do and I was pretty sure my opponents didn't want the things I wanted. We were all scoring.

How to be competitive in your first game: Figure out what everyone else is planning and pursue your own unique strategy relentlessly. If everyone is ignoring doublers, get the doublers every turn and max out your top rail--get the track value upgrader ?-thingy. With the big White track 5+ deep on all doublers, you will rack up the points. Try to keep a coin in reserve in case you get a crack at those engineer guys at the bottom of the board.

RATING: 7.5
2014-10-07

Initial Rating: 7.5 (July 2014)

WOW what a great strategy game! I don’t particularly like the theme (and I didn’t feel it when playing the game) but the game itself is very good. Russian Railroads is a medium weight, worker placement game.

It has a lot of rules, but they are easy to grasp. Besides the game has a great graphic design making easy to explain the rules. The game components and art are also good. It has a satisfying depth and the decisions are very interesting (which action to use, should I use a worker or a coin?) and in no way obvious. You have too few workers to do all the things you want to do. The playtime isn’t that long for a game with such strategy and depth, a huge plus for me. It has a very low luck factor as everything is known from the beginning.

At first blush the game seems to have many paths to victory, but I’m a bit worried that the Trans-Siberian track is very powerful/unbalanced because it is the one that gives you the more valuable (colored) tracks. Also it seems you have to stick with your strategy if you want a big VP payoff so the winner of the game can be decided some turns earlier. The engineers by themselves aren’t that powerful BUT if you get a lot of engineers and score them via industry they can generate A LOT of VPs, that also feels unbalanced. There are almost no player interaction, this isn’t bad for me but I know it bothers some people. 

Bottom line, Russian Railroads is a solid Worker Placement game. I want to play it a lot more. I need to review if the game has multiple ways to win or not. I think I will buy it in the future.

Current Rating: 7.0

RATING: 7.75
Good rail road building game.  Solid elegant design. My only concerns so far are that openings seem scripted - go for enginers, coins, then black rail movement.  Also to do well against experienced players the game forces you to chose a path from round 1 and follow through - and there are really only a set number of paths that can score competitively with experienced players. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Russian Railroads doesn't do much that is new but executes extremely well.  Despite the train theme, RR is NOT a "train game" in the tradition of the 18xx family or the Winsome series.  It is a worker-placement action drafting game.

Probably the game's greatest innovation is its scoring system which relies on multiple different types of "track" on three different routes.  The five different track types have different actions during the action draft, rewarding players who unlock higher level track early as they have little competition for the high level track actions.

I am certain to enjoy this title for at least 5 plays; my concern is that there isn't enough replayability here (only in the conductor draws) to get to the 10-20 play level.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I originally gave this game a rating of 8 after one play.  On subsequent plays, I've found that there are a couple of minor flaws that take away some of the enjoyment of the game.

Early worker placement seems to follow a pattern, and the game is VERY unforgiving.  If a player falls behind in victory points early, they are pretty much out of the game unless the other players make stupid mistakes (not likely).  

There are ways to gain big points at the end of the game to boost a player who is trailing, but with careful play by other players this will probably never happen.  A bit more randomness is needed to keep the scoring tight.  As it is, the game is just an excercise in optimising moves to maximise points... not very much fun.

The train theme, which really doesn't mean much, gives it a little bump in my rating... which is now a 6.

RATING: 8
Wow. This is an impressive mix of worker-placement and medium-weight railroading. Not an economic game, income comes in the form of points, and the scores can run VERY high by game's end. This scratches two of my favorite itches in one box and I look forward to keeping this and playing it quite a bit. 

There are some fresh mechanics here that are not that difficult to grasp. In most railroad games you upgrade your engines and extend your railroad. This abstracts the extension part and puts the focus on upgrading. You upgrade not only locomotives, but also track and industrialization - and you can get specialty engineers that give you unique actions you can perform. I do think that both railroaders and pure eurogamers will find a lot here to enjoy. My rating could easily rise to a 9....

RATING: 8
A strategic worker placement game that has to be learned. Enough depth that an experienced player likely will thrash newbies, lots of elements that need some time to be explained and will fend off all but seasoned gamers. Nonetheless, all elements come together logically and once explained few questions are left.
Discussions show that there are a few strong strategies that need to be countered actively or otherwise a clear victory will happen. The good thing is that there's not a single superior strategy.
Objectively, this is a really strong mid/heavy Euro. In principle, I like it. In fact, my personal enjoyment hinges deeply on playing successfully and as this is one of the strategic games where I usually suck and need a lot of practice to be halfway competitive, I can't rate it higher than a 7 for now.

RATING: 6
A competently done but very vanilla worker placement game. The economic engine is interesting, unique, and multi-dimensioned. (Or maybe it's not even an economic engine - there's no exponential resource growth, just varying allocations of a static resource pool). I'm somewhat tempted to puzzle out good build paths as a solitaire exercise, which is a reaction only a few games with particularly well built engines have managed to elicit (think Homesteaders or Roads and Boats). 

The problem is that the core gameplay and interaction mechanisms are completely stale at this point. Just dealing with a different set of resources is not enough to make the gameplay interesting outside of the solitaire puzzle. If you haven't played too many worker placement games, this is probably a great one to start with. 

Also, it's pretty impossible to see even a hint of the supposed 18xx heritage here. So anyone expecting something like that will be disappointed, this is pure eurogame to the core.

RATING: 8
I like this game, but I have a feeling that it might be nearly "solved."  I thought the trans-Siberian might be dominant, but I was beaten by the Kiev/factory strategy when the third player also went for the trans-Siberian. RATING: 5
Russian Railroads is an elaborate, nonspatial, luckless, themeless (or with a failed theme, take your pick) action drafting game where the game state progresses on separate player boards and interaction happens while drafting for turn order, minor bonuses, actions, and various types of more or less exclusive features. There are only two currencies, refreshing and non-refreshing workers, which are used to pay the variable costs of the action draft. Most actions serve to push markers up four victory point income tracks. Just so no one should think the game isn't very elaborate, the income tracks are variously draped with tinsel of all sorts: tinsel that launches additional markers, tinsel that makes markers pay off more, tinsel that makes tracks pay off more, tinsel that gives one-time currency, action or point payoffs, tinsel that enables or completes the tracks, tinsel that gives you more tinsel, and so on. You win by sticking with a "path to victory", maintaining action efficiency and not having another player trying to hog your spots.

I've seen this described as an 18XX, which obviously couldn't be further from the truth, and as a cube pusher, which is still a bit off. Certainly there's a tangible flavour of "you can get three lemons and one banana for one coconut, or you can get one lemon and one cantaloupe for two coconuts", but as mentioned there's not a lot of currencies to grind through gears of transformation and not so much in the way of setting up feedback loops. It's more of a pure spreadsheet optimization, embodied by a multiplicity of arbitrary additions and multiplications on the individual income tracks. Player differentiation is forced by incidental means, there is no actual shared economy. It's really a simple idea - push those markers to get that income - decked out with a whole lot of tinsel and prize blocks for players to periodically bump for rewards. And it's not what I feel I need more of in my gaming.

This is a solo rating, to be taken with the customary number of salt grains.

Having played this game a good dozen times I am happy that skill in making the right choices is key to the game. Most scoring bonuses give you 20+ points and it is making sure that you have the right bonuses in place for your game that counts and will get you the win.

RATING: 8
Ein Arbeiter Einsetz Spiel.
Durch Einsetzen seiner Arbeiter versucht man seine 3 Bahnstrecken und seine Industialisierung auszubauen, die nach jeder Runde entsprechend Punkte abwerfen. Nach 6 Runden ist der Spuk auch schon vorbei und eine kleine Schlusswertung findet statt.
Um seine Strecken richtig wertvoll zu machen muss diese erstmal mit schwarzen Gleisen gebaut, dann mit sibernen überbaut, gefolgt von braunen -> beigen -> weissen Gelisen, was enorm viele Aktionen benötigt. Alternativ kann man auf Streckenabschnitte gehen und sich Sonderpunkte freischalten oder eben auf die Industrie, die jede Runde auch Punkte akkumuliert. Eine großartige Mischung zwischen allem ist dabei nicht angeraten...
Spiel gefällt mir gut, aber ich brauch noch Übung... Man hat leider immer das Gefühl, dass man mehr machen möchte, aber nicht kann und das Spiel somit viel zu schnell vorbei ist, bevor man überhaupt was erreicht hat ;(.

RATING: 3
What I was hearing about this made it sound like it wasn't going to be my type of game, and hoo-boy is it not. This one IS "just a mish-mash of point-generating elements" with nothing holding it together. It really feels like it was developed by a child, who just decided that this spot right here is the Lucky Frog spot and if you get a hamburger token there you get 20 points. Also this is a train track but THIS one is a Special Train Track and this one is Special Plus and this one is Special Plus Extreme. Glaze it with a varnish of Worker Placement (which isn't an element I dislike, but it's quickly becoming the Lazy Designer's Auction) and you're done. There are strategies an multiple paths and all kinds of stuff to try but, like Tzolk'in or Trajan, none of it is particularly interesting or engaging.

RATING: 8
Very fun game. Well designed. Gives players lots to think about. Good mix of strategy and tactics.  My immediate reaction after two games back-to-back was to think how I can continue to hone my strategy.

The graphic design is superb and is a great example of how well-executed graphic design can enhance the gaming experience.  The scoring rules, which on first blush might seem a bit fiddly, are represented well by the graphic design on the player boards.  Very intuitive.  The artwork is also well done.  Russian Railroads is a nice looking game.

Overall, this is a well-polished game.  Super high-quality.  Will definitely hold a place in my collection for awhile.

RATING: 4
I won my first game but I didn't really enjoy the game that much. It's just not for me because I don't like games that are too "math-y".  It's too much arithmetic for me.  Ok, I have 170 vp + 25 + 18 + 6 x 2 +.... ARGH!!  A lot of decisions come down to which will give me more points but in order to figure that out you have to do like 4-5 calculations in your head, total up all the numbers and then make the decision.  Blahhh. Not for me.

It also feels like each player is really just doing their own thing until someone takes your spot.  But people are usually taking spots not to harm the other person but because they need that spot.  So from a strategic standpoint it doesn't really matter what other players do.  We often found ourselves asking each other "Oh, did you go yet? Is it my turn?" because we didn't need to pay attention to each others turns.

RATING: 7
Not a bad game, but, you need a bookkeeper during the game. Too much counting. After every round, every persons board needs to be scored, and you need to pay good attention, because the counting is not straightforward.

Despite that counting, this is a exciting game, that for some strange reason, keeps you in suspense the whole time. 

It's a bad idea to let one person get away with one extreme strategy. So although you have the reflex to concentrate on your own board only, you should really have an idea what others are trying to do. 

Worker placement for building a point machine, is nothing new, but here it is applied to make up for an exciting game.

The theme stays very deep in the box and hardly pops up in your mind while playing the game

Minus: 2 - 5 - 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An impressively tight package, this is probably the deepest worker placement game you can finish within 90 minutes. Individual strategies are easy to describe but hard to implement, so although there is quite a steep learning curve, you can already have good fun while climbing it. And although it is not a breathtakingly original game, there are still some impressive design novelties in the details, first and foremost the upgrading of the tracks and how it notches up your capabilities.

In spite of all this praise, for my own taste this veers to much towards being a pure abstract, and there is not quite as much interaction as I tend to like in my games. As far as I can tell from this somewhat reserved stance, this seems to be a great game if you are looking for something you can really sink your teeth into – with a dedicated group, this should be a very rewarding game with lots of counter-strategies and feinting going on.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Standard worker-placement game. Not great, not bad either. Unfortunately the theme does not exist and the "Russian" railroad could very easily be, e.g.,  "australian". In fact the implementation of theme is a little strange with all these different rails. There is no interaction between players (as you might expect) besides the competition for an action, although some actions are obligatory at the start of each round, but there is no real tension for them, as everyone knows that they should choose these actions irrespectively of their strategy, so they prepare accordingly.

On the possitive side, the game has easy rules, the gameplay is fast (although prone to AP and a relatively tedious setup) - takes about half an hour for each player - and seems fairly balanced (the not-so-many-as-it-seems-at-first paths to victory are equally strong). 

I don't see why should I pick this over another well-established worker-placement euro, but I would play it occasionally with the right group.

RATING: 9
One of my favorite games from BGG.Con 2013.  It was the first game I played the morning of the con and I was not disappointed.  It wasn't even on my list of games to try before the con started, but I'm glad fate dragged me in that direction.

It's a worker placement plain and simple.  Unlike Agricola where you get penalized for missing items like sheep and veggies, in Russian Railroads a nice balance isn't required.  Pick your path and go nuts.  At the end of the game it's victory point palooza.

The scoring during the game is a bit wacky, but once you understand it, it makes total sense.  Took us a couple of rounds to really understand how you scored each track.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Nếu bạn là một gamer thích những game có hỗ trợ các nước đi thần thánh, những nước đi có khả năng lật kèo toàn bộ game hoặc khiến mọi người phải trầm trồ thì Russian Railroad không phải là game dành cho bạn. RR là một cuộc đua mà chỉ cần bạn hụt một vòng so với đối phương thì coi như kết quả toàn game đã được định đoạt, bạn không có quyền mắc sai lầm trong RR nếu muốn chiến thắng.

Game lấy bối cảnh xây dựng đường sắt ở Nga, nơi bạn là người đang cố gắng xây dựng 3 tuyến đường sắt huyết mạch một cách nhanh nhất và tốt nhất so với đối thủ. Game có 4 đường để ghi điểm khác nhau, và cho dù mỗi đường có những điểm lợi và hại khác nhau thì chỉ cần bạn kiên trì tới cuối 1 đường, số điểm thưởng sẽ là cực kỳ lớn. Điểm của bạn sẽ được tính vào cuối mỗi vòng và nó sẽ liên tục được tăng theo cấp số nhân qua mỗi vòng. Game có 6-7 vòng tùy theo số người chơi và số điểm cuối game của một người chơi thường ở mức 300-400 điểm.

Bạn dùng những công nhân của mình để xây đường sắt, xây các đoàn tàu, xây nhà máy. Bạn dùng tiền để thuê thêm công nhân, các chuyên viên để hỗ trợ cho việc xây dựng và cải tiến các công trình của mình. Đường sắt có 4 cấp độ và đoàn tàu có 9 cấp độ tương ứng với 9 loại nhà máy khác nhau vì khi các đoàn tàu không còn được sử dụng, nó sẽ được rã xác để xây nhà máy. Mỗi loại đường sắt được ghi điểm dựa trên cấp độ của đoàn tàu của bạn, nhà máy ngoài việc cung cấp điểm thưởng mỗi vòng còn cho bạn thêm những hiệu ứng phụ lợi hại và rất hữu ích cho toàn bộ công trình của bạn. Khi bạn phát triển mạng lưới đường sắt của mình đến từng mốc thưởng trên từng đoạn đường bạn cũng sẽ nhận được những hiệu ứng có sức ảnh hưởng lớn. Vậy nên việc phát triển ra sao, đường nào trước, đường nào sau là tùy thuộc vào khả năng của bạn. Việc xây dựng của bạn và người chơi khác không có ảnh hưởng lẫn nhau, nhưng nó lại ảnh hưởng đến những hành động mà bạn có thể làm trong một lượt, vì nếu cả hai đều đi cùng một hướng phát triển thì việc bóp nhau liên tục thường xuyên xảy ra. Bạn cần có những kế hoạch dài hạn và thay đổi kế hoạch ngắn hạn thường xuyên nếu không muốn bị hụt trong cuộc đua đường dài.

Nhiệm vụ ẩn trong game là hạn chế và thường dễ đạt được, cũng như tổng số điểm không chênh lệch nhau bao nhiêu. Cả game mỗi người chơi chỉ có thể có một nhiệm vụ ẩn hoặc không, và nhiệm vụ đó cũng chỉ có thể cho bạn từ 30-40 điểm. Trong khi số điểm một vòng bạn có thể ghi được lại từ 100-200 điểm và hầu như ai cũng có thể hoàn thành được, nên gần như nó không có ảnh hưởng lớn đến kết quả chung cuộc. Đây có lẽ là một điểm trừ, nhưng nếu nhìn theo cách thiết kế tổng thể của game, dường như tác giả muốn người chơi phải thực sự tham gia một cuộc đua quyết liệt và không được mắc sai lầm thì điều này cũng hợp lý.

Nhìn chung RR là một game worker placement tốt, hành động và đồ họa đều gắn kết với theme và yêu cầu khả năng tính toán cũng như tập trung cao của nguòi chơi. Có vẻ game chưa được cân bằng tốt lắm vì có một số đường khá mạnh hơn các đường còn lại, game cần kết hợp với bản mở rộng Germany Railroad để có thể trở nên hoàn hảo. Nếu bạn ưa thích worker placement và race game cũng như những game có thematic tốt thì đây là một game dành cho bạn. Đánh giá base game : 9đ

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A really cool, but rather abstract worker placement game. You are building up train tracks and factories in Russia, only you're not doing it on any kind of map. Instead you have three train tracks on your player board and one factory track.

During the game you try to build up the best engine possible to score points. The first couple of rounds may earn you only a handful of points (you score your board at the end of every round), but it won't be long until your engine truly kicks off. The clue is to pick your strategy and stick to it. If you stray from it, you will lose momentum which can make a huge difference towards the end of the game.

It's very enjoyable, but definitely more for people who enjoy math-y and think-y games.

RATING: 6.5
Very nice worker placement. I love the spaces that require 2 or 3 workers or those that require coins (joker workers). Even the first player debates his move over a blank board because he must balance economy with power. The engineers offer an ever-rotating pair of action spaces that ensures the rounds don't remain stagnant, but it's the locomotives and factories that push the progression of the game rather than Agricola-style round progression. There appear to be so many paths to victory, or at least a different mix of balances between those strategies: four different tracks, deep or shallow, bonus points or track upgrades, engineers or industry. I really like this one.

RATING: 6.5
Provisional rating. I don't like worker placement games that have some really forced moves--in this case, there is exactly one spot for money and one spot for hiring an engineer (for which money is mandatory), so there are some opening moves that are just incredibly strong. So then you end up fighting for turn order and basically it feels like the game is directly _forcing_ you to be a jerk to other players rather than making aggressive play an _option_ or a _strategy_. -- That said, it's got a lot going for it in other ways, so I'll persist playing it for a bit to see if the initial sour taste wears off. I've only been able to play it online as it's been inexplicably out of print for a while.

Next play: ended up basically 2 turns from the end with nothing to do (all the trains were taken, so that was that). Still feels like a particularly mean-spirited game, and while it sucks to be on the receiving end of the meanness, I'm not sure I want to get good enough to be doling out the meanness instead. As with Caylus, it feels to me that unless players are extremely well-balanced in terms of ability, the balance will tip very early and it's basically not much fun to continue playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Based on building the Trans-Sibera rail lines, this worker placement game allows players to expand up to five different rails on a single line. The trick is that the rails have to be developed sequentially, and the later rails are worth more points. Players are also rewarded with extra workers, bonus cards or advantages as they further advance their lines. There are also engineers in the game, which a player can claim and use as their own for the rest of the game. 

This is a very good game, with many paths to victory, and different things to try. This is a very tense game as you try to develop your VP generating income. You don't know if your opponent's strategy will be better than yours!

Strategy wise, the first person to get the 9 locomotive is going to have a good game. If you were racing to get the 9 locomotive and fail in the attempt, then your game will suffer. On the other hand, the Trans Siberian strategy can be effective as well, but you have to be focused, and a few things have to work out, such as your opponents not taking X2 tokens. I've played many games, and I'm still looking forward to more plays. I certainly haven't mastered all the strategies, and sometimes end up on the wrong end of the scoreboard, but still try to get better at this game. 

My only past criticism of this game is that the person with the 9 locomotive will have a strong game. I believe this can be countered by a Trans Siberian strategy, and happy to play both strategies. Currently, my only criticism would be if multiple players are trying to do the same thing, then that may leave another person to potentially run away with the game. 

This game scales well from 2-4 players as well. This would be my vote for game of the year for 2014.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Reinforces how much I dislike WP as a mechanic. It lends itself to higher downtime because if the player takes the action you planned and/or changed the relative value of a variable in a game right before your turn, you need to renalyze the board state to make your decision. When all 4 players do this, it leads to higher downtime. In contrast, games with a card selection dont have this as the value of the card in your hand is usually constant and cant be taken by others. Similar for role selection and simultaneous games where you can still do an action usually and its a faster binary choice of you whether to do it or not, if you can.

This game's negatives: scoring the tracks points is a bit confusing the first time, ignoring the bottom part is very difficult/impossible, and a lot of little bits and pieces. This makes it fiddly but deep, not always the best combination. Positives: the game's action spots were generally well done and is polished and clean for the most part without need to check the rules and such. The math was alright and building up semi interesting. One issue is if a player got ahead, you cant soeasily catch up at all aince the point difference will just grow.

RATING: 6
Russian Railroads is a polarizing title. For those who were expecting to enjoy a new Train game, prepare to be disappointed. The opposite is true for those who were dreading another Train game. RR is a Worker Placement game, and a rather abstract one at that.

RR is complicated, with lots of things to do, giving a variety of paths to victory. One must be flexible when blocked by an opponent and yet stay focused on only a couple of these paths. Lunging for the lowest hanging fruit and only trying to achieve the next nearest VP payout each turn is a surefire way to lose.

Unfortunately, much of what you do in RR makes little thematic sense. The main mechanics of extending your rail lines, improving the trains that run on them, hiring staff, and improving the industry supporting it all I get, but how does one explain the overlaying of grey, brown, creme, and white track? It may sound here like a small aspect of the game, but it is a primary way to gain points.

Victory points skyrocket in RR, possibly reaching over 500! In games with new players versus a veteran I could see this leading to absolutely crushing defeats where the experienced player laps the noobs a couple times.

RATING: 6.5
A big honkin worker placement, NOT a train game. Think Belfort remade by a Feld doppelganger who has the "multiple ways to score victory points" technique down but not the heart or elegance (and loves to have players doing arithmetic). Good experience, might play again, but it felt overdone. I'm also not terribly keen on the unfettered victory point grab-- in that sense, this game reminded me of Royal Palace. Another dampening factor is that I wouldn't play much differently on a second go-round -- I think I chose the right path to score the requisite obscene number of points in rounds 5 and 6. Lack of strategic variation game to game is fine for shorter games, but this clocks in at around 2 hours, and that makes me wonder what I'd get out of a second play. 

(Also of note is that this is one of the more or less silent-strategy games, as opposed to the party-strategy games [Witch's Brew and VivaJava] and the muttering-strategy games [Through the Ages and Twilight Imperium]) 

Things for a newbie to know: always unlock the bonus card ? thingy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
midweight development game. on your development board there are four tracks to develop. on three of four there could be more than two advancing development markers. acquiring higher numbered locomotives allow to score developments to higher numbers on those three tracks. On the fourth track industry tiles allow to climb further. additional workers, bonus scoring tiles can also come from more development. this is a worker placement game and most of the actions are moving development markers. there are locomotive and industry tile actions as well as a money action. money can be used as workers and also engineer tiles can be bought that provide an exclusive action space of your own. there are 6/7 rounds and there is scoring after each round.
the game is relatively fast. since scoring is cumulative it may be difficult to win if you are behind early. I can imagine this game could turn out to be samey after several plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.2
It's a nice worker placement game, with a completely abstract core, that offers you 5 tracks you can advance on, each one with shorter and longer term goals and bonuses.

I enjoyed more the 2 players ones because you can see more what you're doing, you can plan your strategy better (probably because they are shorter too). With more players, you make your move and just pray that not all the other players will pick the spots you want. With 2 players, you can easily see what he's doing and what his interests are; and, in the worst case, you know he will steal only one of your spots. That gives you much more space for planification.

What the games excels on is the decisions. You have to take plenty of decisions, with some risks. 

Still, I'm not in love with this game, as seems to be the case of many many people.

I don't see a lot of variability to grant long term replayability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
首次玩300分 感覺是分差會很大的遊戲
兩人遊戲目前尚未突破400分
雪球般的滾分是很特別的一點
成就感不錯的遊戲
規則挺清楚的，比較沒有容易混淆的地方
規則:
*起始五張獎勵卡打開，額外加分卡暗抽掉兩張，火車頭分類放好(2-9)，工程師照AB分類洗勻，三個A放右邊，四個B放左邊
*每個人七個工人(一個放回盒子)，起始五個工人一塊錢可用，個人圖板最上面放一個1的火車頭(其他沒用到的1收回盒中)，黑鐵軌放第一格，工業軌放對應位置，每個人再拿額外工業軌，獎勵圓片
*隨機發順位卡給每個人，在照反順位拿起始獎勵卡，獎勵資源拿了卡片就移走，起始玩家無獎勵
*每回合輪流放工人，放完工人就執行行動，沒事做就pass根據順位卡計分，七輪後結束，每輪結束計分，錢可取代工人
1.推鐵軌 可分開推不同鐵路的同顏色軌，後面顏色不能超過前面的
2.火車頭 拿數字最小的火車頭放或當成工廠(工廠可拿供應區的)，火車頭放的時候取代其他的，被取代的可放到其他格，如果都滿就放回供應區成為工廠，工廠五個蓋滿後可以任意取代
3. 僱工程師 僱完的成為該玩家的額外行動區
4. 順位格 不能踩在自己順位下面，一個人只能採一格，沒踩的往右推，該工人可以在移動到其他格(第二格先動)，如果首位玩家採第二格且第一個沒人踩，順位不換
*個人圖板如果有符合該標記可獲得獎勵(有火車頭的話要火車頭能涵蓋到)
*每輪計分 每條鐵軌案火車頭涵蓋到的範圍計鐵路顏色分數，還有工廠計分
*之後工程師右移，沒被僱的就移出遊戲，最後一輪順位格會被額外行動取代
*遊戲最後工程師額外加分(數量平手就比數字最大)跟額外加分卡加分
*兩人用另一面圖版，每個火車頭用兩個，工程師3A3B，工人全用，起始六個，起始兩塊錢，可踩在自己的順位格上
*三人火車頭用三個，工程師3A3B，最左邊空著，工人全用起始六個

俄國鐵路
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very good worker placement game that flows well and easily. It's an interesting scoretrack progression mechanism, having to spend actions to push the lower scoring markers along to make room for each successively higher scoring marker. I also liked how there were choices to make between actions that cost 1 worker or the same action with 50% more power that cost 2 workers. It combines freedom and room to move. Plus the variable actions that the game introduces each round, but which can also be "won" for your personal use. In fact, there's lots of things to like. Not so great is that the theme is non-existent ... it really is a pure Euro worker placement game designed to force you to specialise so as to maximise your points and hope that other people leave you the actions you want to maximise that strategy (ie do what others aren't!). Still, if this is your thing, it's very elegant with obvious replay.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Russian Railroads is a worker placement engine-building game.  You're placing your workers to advance of various tracks (literally) on your player board, and you'll score after each round according to how well you've built out the tracks.  Where the game really shines is in the variety of rewards you get for your tracks--building out to certain spots gives you an additional reward, like an extra worker, access to a more valuable track type, or a reward of your choice that can include stuff like end-game bonus cards or better multipliers for the rest of the game.  This stops people from hammering specific tracks, as you have to do a variety to gain these rewards.  There's really a ton of options here, and it just feels really rewarding to try and make everything fit together.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 6
Let's not bang the gaming revolution drums, people. This game does seem solid, however. All of the scoring paths are varied and seem equally viable. In fact, there are so many paths and strategies to scoring that I feel I have to play a bunch more times to even try them all out, let alone refine them. 

Hilarious scoring, with turns near the end of the game piling on the points so fast you'll be whipping around the board like go-karts powered with nitrous oxide. 

This is based on only 2p version so far. 

EDIT: Seems pretty good at first, but there is really no strategic variation game-to-game. The lack of variety is disguised initially by the fact that there are at least four very different paths to victory, and they all seem to have a fighting chance, but that's the thing: they all seem very different but are functionally identical. And once you have explored each of the four strategies, which one you will pursue any given game is almost arbitrary. Not a bad game, but one that does not have replay value beyond 3-4 shots.

RATING: 10
Very nice worker-placement game! People who like to brood plans will find a perfect opportunity to do so. Extremly well done rules, lots of nerve wrecking decisions. Players without plan will lose. Period.

During the game there are still possibilities to modify your initial plans. There are short time and long time planning targets. 

Good variability (random usage of engineers and victory cards during set-up), set-up gives perfect knowledge about the situation and players can immediately start agonizing about the perfect plan. 

This is the best game since Puerto Rico came out - for me, it could be that this one even beats Puerto Rico!

Physically this is very well done and symbology and icongraphy is perfect.

Well done, Hans im Glück!

[Update] This game is good with 4,3 and 2 players! Best is probably 4. This game is so elegant because it allows more development and fun within its 2 hour playing time than other games deliver with double playing time or overly complicated rules.

Another big plus: Clear design for play.
No cards with 4/5th baroque graphics and 1/5th dedicated to 20+ lines of lawyer-style itsy-bitsy 2-point red text on pink background. Good choice of colours (boards and pieces), once you know the game you need the rulebook just to check the VC cards for the first few games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
In this game, players use worker placement to build and improve the rail lines on their player boards. Once everyone passes out of an action round, each player's board produces victory point income based on how far you've reached with your different lines. Once your lines reach certain points on your board, abilities unlock improving your income or giving you a big one time bonus.

The base game alone is very fun, but it does have a few 'best paths' through the game. This is completely solved with the German Railroads expansion, which replaces the player boards from the base game with some that have spots to customize your boards when they are reached with rails.

The worker placement process itself is fascinatingly built where actions require different numbers of workers depending on the strength, and the fantastic tension created by the concept that money can always be used in place of workers, but money is tight, and some of the most important action spaces can ONLY be taken with money.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::star: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I have to say I'm baffled by the compliments this is getting for its 'many paths to victory' and 'overwhelming options'. To me it seems to be a game of choosing one of (at best) a handful of baked-in paths at the outset and spending the rest of the game executing it as efficiently as you possibly can. Indeed, the most thematic element I can detect here is the extent to which the game railroads its players.

Player interaction consists of being aware of the furrows the other players are ploughing so that you can identify the 'hot zones' on the shared worker placement board that you need to prioritise. But even then the game strongly rewards you for hiving off your most vital actions to your player board, via the engineers. The whole thing resembles nothing so much as a complex piece of machinery (complete with prescriptive operating manual), the sole purpose of which is to spit out exponentially-increasing gobbets of victory points. This is what "Euro" means to many people in the 2010s and it makes me sad.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
RRR is a very conservative, but excellently designed and balanced worker-placement Euro game. The interaction between players is limited to the worker placement and snatching good spots away.

In the game you advance on 4 different tracks (3 railroad tracks and 1 "industry" track). Each track is unique in the way it awards VP and gameplay bonuses (early VP vs. late VP; low VP + high bonus and vice versa). At first the game may seem to be a victory point salad (which is somehow true), but what you do is not arbitrary. You rather have to stick to your strategy, which is selecting some tracks to work on (you cannot do all). The order in which you work on the tracks matters very much and the key is to use synergies between those.

There is no luck and only little variation in set up, but  I think trying out different approaches offers some replayability. RRR does not force you to do anything and gives you much freedom in your strategy. It does not do anything innovative, but the gameplay has a very good flow (as a said: a well designed game). The scoring at the  end of each round is  a bit fiddly, but is not detrimental to my enjoyment of RRR. 

Gameplay is pretty  much abstracted from theme. I like how the different tracks represent better developement of the network and the track tokens only represent the frontier of the network (elegant way of representation) and that there is a separation between your network (tracks) and service (trains). But the bonuses for achievements on the track have no thematic representation (why can you only build premium tracks when you have advanced the Transsiberian track?); but these bonuses are the core of gameplay and give rise to the different strategies. The main source of victory points are track and industry advancements (so far thematically sound), but these are accompanied by multipliers, arbitrary end game bonus cards, turn order bonuses and engineer bonuses. While this guarantees the good balancing between strategies, it is a thematic  disconnect and the reason for RRR to be marked a "point salad". However, Soviet Russia is one of the very few settings, in which an economic game without any money makes sense! (These coins are not really money!)

RATING: 6
Players have four interacting development tracks from which they score points. The game is challenging and fun and the components are very good but I've only rated it a 6. To me the game had a problem with its scoring, and that was the arithmetic. And that would limit the groups I could play this with quite a bit.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
2013 was a year of many very good games (as opposed to 2012, a year of a couple of true greats but relatively few others which have become regularly played for us), but this for me is easily the pick of them. What is perhaps most impressive here is that this is an intensely strategic worker placement game with absolutely none of the "I collect these resources, and then do x then y to convert them into victory points" which is found more or less explicitly in just about every other WP game. Instead, everything that happens here is all about scoring VPs, with no intermediate steps - pretty much everything you do is something which sets you up to score more victory points either in the end-of-round scoring phases or the end of the game. And yet what results is not at all a very mathematical or in any sense "solvable" game. There are several paths one can follow in this game, either exclusively or in conjunction with others, and at my current stage in learning the game (yes, I'm still very much learning after probably around 20 plays) it is not at all clear which approach is best.

I'm still quite excited to play this game, even though I played it more than a dozen times in just under a year since owning this, which is pretty frequent for us with any one game (and at all player counts - this works amazingly well with 2 players, another rarity among worker placement games). And I've played it several times since, without getting bored. Perhaps it is possible that, despite each game differing in the available engineers and which scoring cards are in the game, there may eventually prove to be just one path which needs to be followed to get the best possible score. But I don't expect to ever reach this stage, if it exists, so it's really only a theoretical problem for me. This is a game whose appeal is difficult to describe succinctly to someone who hasn't played it, but anyone who enjoys Euros ought to at least give this one a try - it's unique, deep, and tremendous fun.

RATING: 8.4

Approx gametime: 30 - 35 min / player

RATING: 9
Simply love this game. One of the best Euros out there. Multiple paths to victory, lots of things you want to accomplish, limited number of moves and competition with the other players makes this a tense, moderately thinky game, that will test both your planning and adaptation skills. Short versus long-term planning is strong with this game - do you go for quick points from the start, or big points that will require multiple combined steps? And will you be able to accomplish all those steps without someone blocking you on the board? You must also be flexible in this game - the actions you were counting on *will* be blocked by other players (a la Ticket to Ride), and you'll have to improvise and adapt as you go.

If all this sounds good to you - you MUST get this game. It is 90 minutes of pure fun, tension, and very satisfying engine building. 

Of course, this game is not for everyone. There are lots of things you'll want to do, and not enough time to do them all. You must optimize each step, which can be AP prone to some. Also, your plans *will* be tampered with (it's a tight worker placement game) which can be frustrating if you're a long-term planner who puts all their eggs in one basket. You must remain agile, and quickly formulate new plans on the go to do well in this game.

Summary: This is an excellent, excellent Euro for the medium-to-advanced player. It combines everything a Euro gamer could want - suspense, planning, good player interaction, beautiful artwork, and lots and lots of points. And as if that weren't enough, it has some very good expansions by now that just make it even more fun and exciting. Highly recommended game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Not a lot of theme. Not a lot of player interaction beyond the usual worker placement getting in the way, but really everyone is just doing their own thing. Lots of choices. Place your workers to perform an action that lets you trigger another action that lets you trigger another action that bumps up your victory points either immediately or at the end of round scoring or at the end of the game. It's interesting to see who will come out on top through which path. Sort of like a more brain melting Castles of Burgundy, but without the uncertainty and excitement (mild as it is), of the dice rolls. Lots of tiny little decisions all add up to see if you were the most efficient at the end of the game. The fun bit is in chaining up a series of big payoffs in the one turn. Yet, it's a bit sad that that's the "fun" bit.

A solid, well produced game with a delightfully easy to follow rulebook. Engineer distribution, a couple of random bonus cards not starting each game and, of course, different player moves will mix it up a tiny little bit between games. You'll want to play again because you see a way to improve, but all that counting and calculating maybe ultimately makes it more suited as an online or iPad game.

Good to explore, then explore a bit more, then find something a bit more fun, interactive and thematic to play afterwards. Playing against players of different levels of experience is going to be a problem in this one... if you find close games to be more fun. The good news is that you really only know for sure who won right at the end.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
RRR isn't perfect, by all means. It is very dry, the posterchild of pasted theme; unintuivite (colored rails, really?) and the rules are tricky to explain (and understand) because of the multiple points that rarely actually occur in the game, but make a big difference when they do (yeah, I'm looking at you, bonus chip with 2 cards on it). And don't get me started on the scoring system...

Now that this is all out of the way, I can say that RRR is one of my current favourites. It scratches every itch I have about boardgames. The weight is just right, as is game length (60 min), complexity (medium), replayability (huge, at least in the beginning), player interaction (higher than it seems at first sight) and randomness (brought by random setup and other players actions, instead of luck).

Way to go :)

RATING: 9.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Russian Railroads is a fun VP engine game. Focus is rewarded, and if you don't have an idea of how you're going to score points, you won't do well. The game is all about setting yourself up and completing short term goals in order to give yourself long term advantages while competing over the more efficient action spaces. You score the rail and industry tracks every round, which means that scoring in the late game scales up enormously. Actions are very tight in 4 player.

German Railroads drastically improve the variability and strategic diversity of the game. The German boards give you asymmetric boards that emerge as you play, and incentivize pushing multiple tracks. Coal increases the variability, allows you to accomplish more faster, and strengthens industry.

RATING: 9.25

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Played two 3P and two 4P games so far. Russian Railroads is a worker placement game with a minor novelty – it ditches the usual resource conversion system in favour of pushing markers along four different progress tracks. It's a fairly novel system as there are tracks within tracks, and players will need to overcome bottlenecks to push their markers further. The problem, however, is that there are a few dominant paths to scoring. So dominant, in fact, that to deviate slightly will incur penalties. So players will deal with competition for players over certain actions, and maybe adjust their plans a little now and then, but otherwise little in the game will require players to deviate from the scoring paths they have chosen. 

Something also unique to Russian Railroads is that scores [i]will[/i] snowball to absurd amounts by the end of the game. It was pretty exhilarating the first time it happened, but the novelty wore off with progressive games because the scoring system was so prone to miscalculation. 

Once the game's appeal of exploring the few scoring paths available wears off (and it will eventually), you're left with a fairly standard worker placement game.

----
1 play, 4er

Very good worker placement game, with a train theme thinly pasted on -- didn't feel it at all, although that might be because it was a learning game. There is a LOT going on here, a LOT to learn at once! 

The game was very enjoyable, but also had a bit of an odd engine-building mechanic: at the start of the game, you're scoring just a handful of points each round. On that final turn, you could be scoring over a hundred! Was there even any point in scoring those few little VP early on? Is there any hope at all of catching the player who manages to get ahead? Also, counting those VP every turn was a little annoying!

I'd like to play it again, but I may prefer Snowdonia if I'm looking for a train-themed worker placement game?!

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
Each player has their own board, with space for factories, and 4 rail tracks (to 4 different cities). On each track, use a track token to mark the progression of your rails (different colored marker for each type or rail). Some interesting twists:
- The different track types must be built in a specific order (black, gray, brown, white). Later tracks may never be advanced further on the track than the earlier tracks.
- On each track, as the track head advances, you cross several thresholds that provide awards: the ability to start a new color of track, victory points, bonus tiles, etc.
- Each track line can have one (two for the first rail) engine associated with it; the size of the loco(s) determines how far down the track you actually score VP

The central board has (almost) all the locations for placing workers. Each location requires 1-3 workers (of one player; played all together). Players, who start the game with 4 workers, will take turns using a location. These provide a variety of abilities, for example:
- advance 1 or 2 track heads by 1-3 spaces
- acquire an engine or factory; engines are allocated to rail lines, while factories (the reverse side of the tile) are placed on your factory line.
- earn 2 coins
- take 2 temporary workers
- jump ahead in turn order
- acquire an engineer, which has a unique power and becomes a worker-placement location only for you
- get an end-game bonus VP card

Each round ends when all players have passed on placing/using workers. Then, score VP for each track line. On each line, only spaces as far down the track as the loco level will score. Each track type scores VP for every space from its track head back to where the next color of track starts. Track types built earlier (e.g. black) score less/space than later tracks (e.g., white).

After 7 rounds, the game ends; most VP wins!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I actually really liked this game up until about halfway through it.  Loved it, even.  I'm a huge worker placement fan and loved the mechanics of this game.  It seemed well designed with a lot of options, interesting player interaction, and thinky ways to play.

The friend who introduced this game to us even described it as a game where instead of someone screwing you over with a selection they really just divert you but you still have a decent option available to you.  Boy howdy was he wrong!

About halfway through I already started to see the mold on this game's design.  That being that it had a serious runaway leader problem.  I was even the beneficiary of this design mistake but I don't like feeling like I've run away with a game halfway through.  The tension is gone in those situations and the game becomes less exciting.  I was able to "min-max" my board and by ignoring certain tracks and actions was outscoring my opponents by 30-50 points a turn.  By the final turn I was 80 points ahead of my next closest opponent and "lapped" both of the other two players (scored over 100 points more than them).

The final turn is what really soured the game on me though.  During this game a player accumulated 6 out of 7 of the engineers in the game.  These engineers are powerful enough, they give the player who acquires them an action that only they can use.  However, without acquiring these I was still able to make optimal action selections and outscore my opponents every turn.  My whole mentality was that acquiring these engineers wasn't overwhelmingly beneficial to this player because a. he was still limited by his worker total and b. not all of the engineers provided optimal action spaces and many went unused for several turns.  I repeat, boy howdy was I wrong!

Well, not necessarily wrong, I just didn't know about one particular piece in the game that would gift-wrap the win for this player after a 2-3 hour mental exercise.  There are four available factories in the game that let you score 9-16 points for each of your engineers.  With 6 engineers and two of these factories the player was able to score 114 points in the middle of a round when our end-of-round scoring capped out at 90-100.  This is insane.  Sure, we were punished for allowing him to accumulate so many engineers but the punishment did not fit the crime.  Nobody wants to spend 2-3 hours playing a game where even with a comfortable lead they can be buried by one turn, to me that is a huge design problem.

I have to admit (begrudgingly) I did enjoy most of the rest of the game and knowing now about this quirk I would play it again taking care to make everyone at the table aware.  But I would also be remiss without criticizing this game for what I think is just a bad design element.  Maybe games like this exist for players that like the ability to take advantage of broken hidden mechanics like this, but I'm not one of those players.

Lately this game is getting a lot of press and every time I see it I think about how much I enjoyed (loved, really) the parts of the game that weren't the broken points-for-engineers tiles.  I definitely want to play this game again (and may even purchase a copy) but never again will I let it go unknown how important not letting one player accumulate the majority of engineers is.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Helmut Ohley and Lonny Orgler are known primarily as 18xx designers, and I rather suspect that Russian Railroads started as an exercise in abstracting out the 18xx train rush. If so, I'd love to know if it was their idea to strip out rusting -- i.e., that which makes a train rush interesting -- or if it was Hans im Gluck relentlessly polishing all the sharp edges away in the name of being family-friendly.

The gist is that players score points by advancing markers along three tracks, representing railways; however, points are only scored (and most special spaces activated) if the player has a suitable locomotive assigned. The 2-trains activate the first two spaces on the track, and have to be purchased before the 3-trains, which activate the first three spaces, all the way up to nine.

Trains are acquired and tracks are built with a lot of banal worker placement: there's not much train DNA left, despite the theme. There's competition for the central spaces, sure, but it hews dangerously close to being a solo optimization puzzle. That said, as a worker placement engine building blah blah blah it is truly excellent, and if that kind of game interested me I would be thrilled. It is probably the best such since Tzolk'in, but with a longer-term focus due to the necessity of committing to a strategy relatively early.

7.5 -> 6 (this just isn't the sort of game I like these days, and even the smoothest multiplayer-solitaire worker placement game is still what it is)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
This game was quite high on my list. It's one of the heaviest titles by HiG. It's from the designers of [thing=76417][/thing], an other train game (with a different theme though) that I enjoy quite a bit. And this was one of the titles getting a lot of buzz this year, so I had to grab a copy.
The rules are the way you've come to expect from HiG: VERY clearly structured, written and layed out. One read of the whole book is enough to explain the game straight away with no questions coming up. Very, very well done. And luckily the game is just as well developed. Everything is very clear and very straight forward. But the combinations do offer multiple strategies to explore, lots of different ways to score points.
I was also pleasantly surprised by the duration which was about 1h45 with 4 new players.
So far this is my favorite of the 2013 Essen crop and I look forward to exploring it more in the near future.

RATING: 6
This is the type of worker-placement game I enjoy. Russian Railroads provides a tight focus on just a few point-scoring opportunities harnessed by limited resources. This is opposed to the contemporary Feldian school of design where there are a multitude of scoring opportunities scattered all over the board.

What's more, Russian Railroads plays relatively quickly. I appreciate the recent trend in many worker-placement games towards shorter playing times. Glass Road and Keyflower also come to mind here.

Finally, Russian Railroads has a clever system that is reminiscent of base-running. You can't advance one runner (track type) past another, but the more you can advance the runner furthers back, the more points you score. Clever.

Subsequent plays only reinforce my feelings about the game even if there are certain set strategies that emerge. As with similar games of its type, player interaction is low and so there is a limited shelf-life to the game. As people settle into their preferred method of scoring, you'll need to spice things up by either moving on to another game or adding the expansion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
First Impression:

A simple worker placement game, refined and polished to an outstanding level.

There is little I can say about this game's core mechanisms. The basic worker placement works very well, with great player interaction that feels just right and tight decisions. There are clear diverging paths of strategy, and players may pursue many of them at varying order in their quest for victory. Most importantly however, this game flows extremelly well, is easy to teach and play and forces constant tough decision making. In short, it's elegant, very elegant. Finally, there is a clear sense of progress and the game doesn't really outstay its welcome, feeling just right in duration.

The game does little to boost its theme, but despite this and myself (I don't really like the train themes in general) the theme carries, even if weakly. The multiple railway layers and the gradual railway progress are intuitive enough, as well as the engines and the gradual introduction of better ones. There are great abstractions of course, but overall it's much less dry than I first assumed.

Overall this is a euro that fits almost perfectly to my preferences. It has simple yet very strong and rewarding mechanisms and a theme that manages to carry through. I look forward to play it more.

RATING: 7.5
If you're expecting a standard train game, this ain't it.  There's no route building, no pickup and deliver, there's no competition to get anywhere.  It's a multiplayer solitaire worker placement point salad game... and it's brilliant.

You're buying trains, factories and engineers and laying track.  There's all sorts of bonuses that trigger when you've got track built to them and a train that can reach that far.  And once you've started building your track, you can come along behind it and start upgrading it... which means it'll score better.  There are three main strategies, go for industrialization and build factories, hire all the engineers, or build tracks and trains.  Although to be honest, you'll probably do a little of everything.

Things start out slooooooow.  But by the end of the game you'll be speeding along.  The game really does a great job of making you feel like you're accomplishing something by the end of the game.  This isn't a game where you feel like your last turn is just eking out a point or two.  About a third of my score came from the last turn.

This was one of the best new games I've played in a while.  I fell in love with this one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
About [thing=144733][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=144733][/thing] is a strategic work placement game in which players try to build their points machine better than the others. Once the set up is done, there is no more randomness in the gameplay, allowing players more control over their destiny and paths to follow.

There is a good learning curve working here as there are many options and interconnections between several parts, as well as timming to make some advancements and/or build factories and getting new locomotives or upgrading them. This all means that an experienced player will likely trounce any new player and won't even have a hard time doing it, as a proven strategy will likely work again and again, with only a few minor changes and corrections along the way.

The production value is good: the bits are nice and the tokens do their job. The art is merely OK but the graphic design is pretty neat, making the options clear and playing easier.

Replay value can be an issue: the only random part is the order of the engineers during the set up. Therefore, plays can follow the same beaten path and the offer few new decisions - it will be up to the players to truly challenge themselves.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
Play will last for a given number of rounds (7 or 6). At the start every player will have 5 workers to use, but can gain more by unlocking one or two during play, or hire 2 more for a round, using one of the options on the board.

During the turns, players will allocate their workers and use the spaces on the board. Several advance the development of tracks - moving black, grey, brown, cream or white markers. Others will advance the industry of the player, get money, extra points, new factories and new trains. It is possible to hire the available Engineer, and use the next two that later will be up for hire.

All spaces, except for one that allows players to advance a black or gray marker, once used are blocked for the whole turn. Hired Engineers give extra places for positioning workers, only available for the players that have them.

Turn order is also changed by the use of workers - the ones used to do this can he reallocated once everyone have passed.

A player without workers and money (as money can be used as workers) must pass - receiving some points if playing in 2nd, 3rd or 4th. Once everyone have passed and the workers in the turn order (if any) reallocated, the round is over. Players tally their points - gray, brown, cream and white markers are worth points, if their positions are "activated" by a locomotive - for instance, a track with a 6 Locomotive is activated up until the sixth spot on it. Some tracks give extra points and the industries, accordingly to how advanced they are, also scores each round.

Once the game ends, everything will be scored as normal, as each end of round, with the addition of goal cards, if the player has any of them, and the Engineers: the player that hired more of them will gain 40 points and the second, 20. 

The player with the most points will be the winner!

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
There are plenty of them:
- Where to allocate workers;
- What tracks to develop;
- Deciding between Locomotives and factories;
- Picking bonus tiles;
- Etc.

Players usually need to focus in a strategy, meaning that, although there are many choices on the board (and Engineers), prioritizing give the player a way to restrict options, though being able to seize good opportunities can provide an edge. Alongside prioritizing, timming also puts weight on the decisions, as unlocking some tracks, taking early some bonus, grabbing first a goal or an Engineer, also counts a lot.

Therefore, although the options available aren't truly complicated, the many aspects involved in each of them, and the lasting effects of them, adds difficulty to decide.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Pacing - plays usually lasts around or under 90 minutes, even when having 4 players;
- Nice range of options and decisions;
- No random aspects, allowing for strong strategies;
- Excellent graphic design and nice components.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- No direct interaction;
- Learning curve makes playing with people of different level of experience dicey;
- Game can be over before the game is actually over;
- Dry and with little theme;
- Replay value can be an issue.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like in soviet Russia where the trains ride you. Mainly because not even for a single moment I felt like I was building a railroad network or even a train game. I was, more, railroading - as the tracks where already laid there, and I only had to paint inside the lines.

Now, this can be a little harsh, as [thing=144733][/thing] is a pretty solid strategic game: you have plenty of options, no random elements once the game starts, relevant decisions and all put in a reasonable playing time with a fine pacing. Yet, [thing=144733][/thing] is also the sort of dry, solitaire and themeless game that I hardly can get into anymore - I still like very much some of these, but seems that the room for them in my preferences is getting smaller.

In the end, [thing=144733][/thing] has several strong positives, and even some of the "bad news" can be seem as positive to many players, specially those that like to develop their strategies and make their machines work with little interference, except some blocking. You probably know by now if you will like it, or not or be indifferent. I'm in this last group.

RATING: 7.5
Russian Railroads is a medium heavy, almost luckless worker placement game. Its rulebook is so well written and the graphic design of its components is so well thought out, that it is remarkably easy to learn and teach. I really like this type of optimization game, and RR is no exception. My only concern with this game, is that it has limited replayability because of its deterministic and almost perfect information. That is why I have no desire to own it, even though I would gladly play it again.

RATING: 5
An elegant, well-implemented but bog-standard worker placement VP optimisation game.

No relevance at all to railroads, except the art, and the mechanisms (at least with two) discourage interaction. There were always enough action spaces to get what you wanted, and the challenge was in fiddling with your own player board.

If you like dry VP optimisation games where your opponent doesn't mess with your plans, this is the best out there. I was engaged while playing it, but felt it was all a bit purposeless and there was no need to repeat the exercise.

Played once, left on the shelf for a year. When we discussed playing it again, I realised I'd rather do two hours of work than endure this again.

RATING: 6
I like the idea of a worker placement/train game, but truth be told, this is really just a worker placement game with some tech trees on it.  The game could have been about anything (or even any railroad; there's nothing in the game that screams "Russia!" at me), but they chose this theme to make it seem more unique.  Most of the actions seem pretty basic, but adding the personal/variable action spaces make each game a little different.  I'm interested in playing the game some more, but it wasn't quite the game I was expecting it to be.

UPDATE: It seems like the engineers are critical to running a good game here.  That seems discouraging, since players have to waste moves to take suboptimal engineers, only so someone else doesn't wind up running away with the end-game and technology track bonus scoring (or maintaining a low locomotive so as not to discard any 1 factories).

1 play: 3 players
2 plays: 4 players

RATING: 6
I'm not sure I really get Russian Rails. It feels like pulling random levers on a slot machine. Sure, you can see the different forks on the road pre-printed on the game's player board and bonus tokens, but I have literally no idea what story it is trying to tell, nor what to look for when it comes time to decide what direction I should go.

It's just a flat out weird game. You'll notice I have played it quite a bit though, which seems odd considering the critical tone of my last statement. Well, like pulling the arm down on a slot machine, I can't in any way say it's not fun, regardless of whether or not I'm connecting the dots. I don't get it, but I'm a sucker for the lights, so I'll probably play it a whole lot more. regardless of my complete lack of comprehension.

RATING: 8.5
Based on one play, I'd say this is a very solid worker placement game. Those who find games like Agricola too depressing because of their fight-to-keep-your-head-above-water nature will enjoy this game because progress is only ever forwards, and exponentially so. 

My only worry is about whether repeated play would find the same sorts of strategies winning all the time. It seems like there are a lot of different things to go for, but it's tough to balance these sorts of games. On one play, I have no concrete basis for this worry, but I'd hate to buy the game and find that it's like Le Havre, where most of the choices are illusory and the only viable endgame is steel-centric (except instead of steel, here it would be e.g. everyone going for white rails, and the person who also gets the most engineers bonus wins, or something).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Rating updated to reflect my opinions of the game after playing the German Railroads expansion.

------------------------

I've been able to play this a couple times more recently now and I really enjoy it. It is yet another worker placement game in my collection but has some pretty interesting elements in it that other games in my collection don't have.

Some of the things that I enjoy are the the conductors that come out during the game and the fact that you can utilize their special abilities on the main board or can purchase them to personally gain their benefit from round to round. I also like the upgrading of your trains at they are required to reach objectives further and further down your tracks. Slowly upgrading the types of rail that you have in each of your individual tracks and points that they each give. The bonuses and industries that you can build... and how all of these work together, or if you're like me, against what you're trying to accomplish.

This is very much a euro-game through and through with tons of ways to pick up victory points. The strategy comes from figuring out the most effective way of picking up those points and evolving your strategy over time. Because I haven't played it that much and I tend to like to explore strategies in these games, I've generally be horrible at the game, but that hasn't stopped my enjoyment of it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

However, it is quite serious and dry and only the last three turns are really interesting (first turns are rather 'programmed' since options are limited and the game systems takes some time to get into gear. See also: Macao). 

Probaby in the same league as the heavy Felds. It will take a few more plays to decide whether this one will push Macao or Trajan from my shelf.
